# Learning The Javascript Language

> JavaScript is one of the most popular programming languages in the world, used to create dynamic websites along with a host of other applications. And like any language—whether spoken or for programming—it takes time and practice to become conversant. In this course, Joe Chellman teaches you how to “speak” JavaScript by familiarizing yourself with the most commonly encountered topics and concepts,

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208) | Beginner | 401K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

## Table of Contents

### Chapter Overview

- **1. Introduction** (3 videos)
- **2. 1. Getting Started** (5 videos)
- **3. 2. Variables and Types** (6 videos)
- **4. 3. Objects, Arrays, and More** (9 videos)
- **5. 4. Operators and Control Structures** (8 videos)
- **6. 5. Iterating with Loops** (4 videos)
- **7. 6. Functions** (10 videos)
- **8. 7. A Few More Advanced Pieces** (6 videos)
- **9. Conclusion** (1 videos)

>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/become-conversant-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=7)** But now with JavaScript, the web is extremely programmable and because of its popularity, JavaScript can be used to program much more than just the web.

>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/become-conversant-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=24)** You'll begin to understand most of the major pieces of JavaScript, such that you'll be able to read and write enough code to get started on your own projects.

#### Exercise files and Codespaces
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Tutor] The exercise files for this course include everything you'll need to follow along, because this is a course about learning how to read JavaScript, as well as write it, the files are mostly in the form of transcripts of what you see, as well as some examples for copying and pasting.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=16)** There are folders for each chapter, and each video containing anything you'll need to try what you've seen.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=22)** You can open these and read through them at your own pace.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=25)** The files are provided in a GitHub repository, which gives you many options for how to use them.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=33)** You can download a ZIP archive of the files to your machine, unzip it, and open them in your favorite text editor, we'll discuss editors shortly, and if you've used GitHub before, you should feel free to fork the code to your own account if you like.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=47)** However, there's a new and very powerful way to interact with this courses files that allows you to follow along exactly, using everything I'm using in exactly the same way.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=56)** GitHub Codespaces.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=59)** Codespaces are virtual machines running on GitHub servers that give you Visual Studio Code, a powerful text editor with the same plugins I'm using, and an installation of Node.js, a JavaScript environment that runs in Visual Studio codes built in Terminal, so you can do everything in the same browser window.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=76)** In order to use codespaces, you'll need a free GitHub account From the course page On LinkedIn Learning, you'll see a button that will take you to the exercise files on GitHub, labeled GitHub Codespaces here, and it will allow you to create a codespace on Main.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=93)** This means you're creating your own individual codespace from the code on the LinkedIn Learning GitHub account, Main is the name of the branch of the code you're using.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=103)** The version control software under the hood is called Git, and branches are used to track different revisions to code over time.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=110)** In this course, there's only one that's called Main, but in other courses, you might see many.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=117)** You can also create a codespace from the main exercise files page, here under Code, you would click Codespaces.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=125)** Now I already have one, which is showing up right here, for you, there will be a Create Codespace on Main button.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=132)** I could also create additional codespaces using the plus button up here.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=136)** Codespaces can be started from the LinkedIn Learning Repository here, or if you decide to fork the code to your own account, which means you have a totally separate copy of the code attached to your GitHub account to play with however you like, you can create a codespace from there, all the configuration for the codespace will come with the forked code.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=155)** I'll click on my codespace name here to launch it.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=160)** It will take a minute or so to load, depending on your connection speed, and once it's finished, you'll be in an online version of Visual Studio Code, and you can open any of the course files, which again, are transcripts and runable examples.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=174)** Here under the Files tab, you can see all the chapter numbers here, and these are the videos associated with each chapter that have exercise files.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=183)** So I can open any of these to see a transcript, which is what you'll see in most cases, and every once in a while, there will be another file or two as well.
>

>

>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=213)** I'm going to click the little trash button to leave me with just the one that I need, and we'll be running the code using Node, which you can start just by typing "node" there in the terminal.

> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/exercise-files-and-codespaces?u=76281980&t=239)** If that happens, you can choose to pay for more time, these prices are shown in US dollars for me, or just wait until the beginning of the next month when you get a new, free block of 60 hours to play with.

### 2. 1. Getting Started

#### Syntax: Learning to read and write
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the next several chapters of this course, we'll be going over the syntax of JavaScript.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=4)** This is not going to be a complete coverage of every single element of JavaScript syntax because the goal here is to allow you to get conversant in JavaScript.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=13)** Just as with any other spoken language, you won't be totally fluent in a matter of a few hours, but you will be able to read code like this which is loaded with non-executable comments and some actual functionality and you'll be able to make sense of it.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=27)** You'll understand control structures, the way we make decisions about what to do and when.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=34)** You'll understand the different kinds of data types that are available, like numbers, lists, and so on.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=40)** You'll know how to use variables which are places to store data.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=44)** You'll understand comments and white space which help us understand what we've written and make sure we can actually read it.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=51)** Generally, you'll have a pretty solid understanding of how the language is put together, enough that you can start working with it on your own.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=57)** That's the goal.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=58)** Not perfect fluency, but to become conversant.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=61)** Programming languages are a lot like human spoken languages.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=64)** You need to learn the grammar in order to use it, but that's not enough to become totally fluent.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=69)** You need to put in a lot of time reading the language, writing the language, generally using it in whatever way you want to.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=76)** With human spoken languages, of course, you could simply speak it with your voice and get a lot of use from it.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=82)** But with JavaScript, you need to be able to read and write it.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/syntax-learning-to-read-and-write?u=76281980&t=85)** And after going through this course, you should be able to read and write code and begin to do what you need to using the language.

>
> **CLI Commands:** make (3)
> **Cross-References:** in the next (1)

#### Versions of JavaScript
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Programming languages go through revisions over time just like apps do.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=4)** Bugs are fixed, new features are added.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=7)** In JavaScript, the feature editions are what people tend to get excited about, which isn't a surprise really.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=12)** Let's talk briefly about the versions of JavaScript that you'll hear about.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=16)** Though JavaScript is the language's most commonly used name, the body that maintains its standards is ECMA International, so versions are named accordingly.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=24)** ECMAScript 5 or ES5 is the version that runs on all modern web browsers really since 2012 or so.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=31)** There are older versions, but this one is a general baseline.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=34)** And until fairly recently, ES5 was the version people would usually learn first since there were still enough places where you couldn't expect anything newer to run safely.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=43)** Indeed, that was the version of JavaScript mainly covered in prior versions of this very course.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=49)** As of now, we're supporting the next version, ECMAScript 2015, also known as ES6, as the baseline.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=55)** There are a lot of new features in this version, and browser support is generally good enough to use in any modern environment safely.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=63)** As you might guess, there are other newer versions as well finalized pretty much every year that continually add more features and make the language feel new and shiny all over again.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=72)** For the most part, ES5 and ES6 make up the lion's share of JavaScript in common use.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=78)** If you want to use the features of a newer version of JavaScript without sacrificing compatibility, there exist tools called transpilers, which can take your newer, less compatible JavaScript and convert it to ES5 or whatever prior version you need, so it can run on older web browsers.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=94)** The most popular of these is called Babel, available at [babeljs.io](https://babeljs.io).
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=99)** If your JavaScript journey will go into more recent features and you need to add support for older browsers with the code you write, you'll want to be familiar with transpilers.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=108)** The main takeaways for now are that JavaScript is constantly evolving.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/versions-of-javascript?u=76281980&t=112)** We are covering ECMAScript 2015 or ES6 as the baseline here in this course, but everything we cover in this course remains useful as JavaScript continues to evolve.

#### Your friend the text editor
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=0)** - In this video, I'm going to extol the virtues of a good text editor giving you some reasons why you might actually want to have one in your toolkit whatever your level of experience.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=9)** If you're a web designer coming to JavaScript, you might have an editor you like for HTML and CSS and that editor will probably be great for JavaScript as well.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=18)** I've been teaching new developers for years and I'm continually impressed with how many high quality free editors are available.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=26)** We'll mostly talk about the features that are noticeable when you're working on very small projects with a small number of files and not a lot of code.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=32)** Editors can of course work with projects including thousands of files but they're great for small things you'll be looking at when you start as well, and it's worth noting that using GitHub code spaces for this course means that you'll have a nice editor without any further effort.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=46)** We'll be looking at editors for other projects that you might work on in the future.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=51)** The first thing that you'll notice when using a code editor is syntax highlighting the editor uses colors and styles to mark the different kinds of code to help us know what we're looking at at a glance.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=61)** So that means the difference between this and this.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=66)** syntax highlighting basically takes the grammar of the language and makes it visible.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=71)** Understanding what we're trying to do is much easier when we can see this much detail.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=75)** Code completion is the next big one.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=79)** Your editor saves you typing by giving you options for how to finish what you're typing based on what it knows about JavaScript and about the code in the files you're working on.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=88)** So for example, here in a empty JavaScript file, if I start typing console, I get completions on the things that JavaScript knows I might want.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=100)** Most editors these days have some awareness of complete projects and can draw on that knowledge to save you work.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=107)** So that means you'll get code completion not just for JavaScript itself, but anything you or your team have defined as well.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=114)** Editors can also help with the tedious parts of writing code, like managing special punctuation.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=118)** JavaScript uses a lot of parenthesis, quotation marks braces, and other punctuation that often come in pairs.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=125)** You can see a lot of those just here at the beginning of this massive file.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=129)** Pretty much all editors will automatically autotype these for you and auto delete them if you type too many.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=135)** So if I start to define a function, I know we don't necessarily know what that is, but just bear with me for a moment.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=141)** If I type that first of my pair of parentheses, the editor gives me the other one automatically.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=148)** You can see my cursor is between them, but I've only typed one of them.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=151)** And if I change my mind and just hit delete one time, they both go away.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=156)** This works for other kinds of punctuation as well.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=159)** There are also features to help you avoid common errors, reformat code, so it's more readable, but still executes correctly and more.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=168)** Eventually, you might even step up to an integrated development environment or IDE, which has all the features of a great editor but also adds features to help professional and enthusiastic amateur programmers get their work done faster.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=181)** I said there are a lot of great options.
>

>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=188)** Visual Studio Code is from Microsoft.
>

>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=194)** It's updated almost every month with lots of useful features and is very popular right now.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=199)** It's also the editor that GitHub Code Spaces uses so it's what you'll see in this course.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=204)** Sublime Text is another very popular editor.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=208)** It's not free, but it can be used without payment for a long time.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=211)** Unlike many other current options, which are kind of like high-powered web apps running on your machine, sublime is native which should make it feel faster to use.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=219)** Brackets is another open source option, founded by Adobe but now developed by its own community.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=226)** It's targeted at web designers with support for CSS pre-processors out of the box, among other things.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=231)** And of course, it's also great for JavaScript.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=234)** If you're wondering what I use in my daily work, I mostly work in PhpStorm, an IDE that supports JavaScript and Php equally well, which is important for me.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=244)** It's a little heavier to work with than these other editors.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=247)** It takes a very little bit of configuration for any project that I add to it, but once it's set up, I get so much useful help that I miss it every time I use another editor for Serious Work.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=256)** As you grow in your skills as a JavaScript programmer, you might consider looking at WebStorm, Jet Brains JavaScript only IDE and see if you like it.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=265)** My recommendation for now is that you pick one of these editors, download it and stick with it for at least a couple of weeks, learning its keyboard shortcuts and maybe even reading some documentation so you can really get the most out of them.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=277)** You'll probably start to recognize the things that you like and what you wish you could change.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=281)** If the editor doesn't work a way that feels natural to you, you can try another one.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/your-friend-the-text-editor?u=76281980&t=285)** There's lots of options available, and they're all great.

>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/declaring-and-assigning-variables?u=76281980&t=352)** There are a few words you can't use for your variable names because they're part of JavaScript itself.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/declaring-and-assigning-variables?u=76281980&t=357)** They're called Reserved Words.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/declaring-and-assigning-variables?u=76281980&t=359)** From what we've seen so far, you wouldn't be able to use "var" as a variable name because JavaScript already claimed it.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/declaring-and-assigning-variables?u=76281980&t=364)** If you're curious, there is a list of reserved words available on the Mozilla JavaScript reference site, and of course, if you're using a nice code editor, it will highlight those words for you so you won't accidentally use them.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/declaring-and-assigning-variables?u=76281980&t=376)** So that's an overview of variables which are the way that we give names to data.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/declaring-and-assigning-variables?u=76281980&t=381)** If we're thinking of JavaScript like a spoken language, they're sort of like nouns.

#### Strings
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to look at strings.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=3)** A string is character data.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=5)** That means things like sentences, words, single letters, even numbers or punctuation.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=11)** Basically, it means anything between quote marks.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=14)** So for example, I could write, "this is a string".
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=20)** When you formulate a string like this, it's actually called a string literal.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=24)** Literal here basically means you're making data of some type using the simplest notation possible.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=30)** So making a string literal means I'm putting string data in quotes.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=34)** There are other less convenient ways to make strings as well, but this is what we use 99% of the time.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=40)** A string doesn't have to be a sentence.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=43)** It can be a number inside quotes.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=45)** So, if it's a string of 1, 2 instead of 12, that's a string.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=52)** These are two different things as far as JavaScript is concerned.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=56)** You can make strings using these double quote characters.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=60)** You can also make strings using single quote characters.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=63)** Like 'this is also a string'.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=67)** Whichever you use, you need to begin and end the string using the same character.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=73)** So I can't do this, This is a string, with a single quote at the beginning and a double quote at the end.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=79)** If I try to execute that, I get a syntax error.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=82)** Sometimes you're going to need to write a string that will have a quotation mark inside it.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=87)** This comes up a lot actually, if you're working with HTML in JavaScript.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=91)** So if you're writing a piece of HTML like this, this would be an anchor tag.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=96)** It works because I put the single quote at the beginning and the end with the attribute, in this case, the href attribute as a double quote.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=107)** This is one of the many times where syntax highlighting would be helpful, so, let's open a file in the editor.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=118)** Here is the transcript file that's associated with this particular video.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=122)** So here's what I've typed so far, and here are some other bits.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=126)** The color scheme that I'm running here shows strings in a sort of salmon color, so I can see whether a string is enclosed correctly.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=134)** If it is, everything stays the salmon color, and if not it'll end up turning white, or some other non-string color.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=145)** The double quotes with the single quotes inside works fine and the opposite works as well.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=151)** So in this case, I have a single quote here inside the string, but because there are double quotes on the outside, it works great.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=159)** You will at some point need to include quote marks of both kinds inside a string, like this, for example, here's Joe's favorite string.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=168)** I have both a single quote mark here and double quotes here.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=173)** So what JavaScript sees, we can see this based on the coloring, is a string, followed by something that is unknown, and is going to make JavaScript mad, and then another string.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=185)** To fix this, so it's all one string, I need to use a backslash character like we see here.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=192)** This is what's called escaping the string.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=194)** For each quote mark that's causing trouble, I can add that backslash.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=202)** Once I've done that, this is formatted correctly and will execute properly Just to prove it works, let's try this down in the console.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=210)** I'll copy and paste this and you can see that works.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=215)** Different environments will give you this result in different ways.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=218)** Node is showing me this version enclosed in back ticks, you may see it with other kinds of variations on the quotation marks, as well.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=228)** Back slashes let us do one more helpful thing, break strings up into multiple lines like you can see here.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=234)** If we want to write a string across several lines we can do it that way, making sure that the only thing that comes after the back slash is a return, and then end the string with that final quotation mark that matches the first one.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=246)** For something this short, it doesn't really matter, but when you need to edit long runs of text, this can be helpful.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/strings?u=76281980&t=252)** And that is a quick introduction to strings.

#### Booleans for what is true
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to talk about booleans, that is true and false and why booleans matter to us.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=7)** You may know from the way people talk about computers, sort of colloquially, that computers deal with ones and zeros, on and off, true and false, that sort of dichotomy.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=17)** Boolean is a data type that can capture true or false for us.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=22)** In JavaScript, a boolean is either true, spelled this way, or false, spelled this way.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=29)** They need to be spelled exactly like this, all lowercase, and of course, spelled correctly in order for JavaScript to know that you mean true and false.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=38)** If I write true with a capital T, JavaScript is going to think you mean a variable by that name and I can see I get a reference error when I try that.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=46)** Incidentally, because it's so similar to that existing keyword, you would do really well to avoid naming any variable true like this.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=54)** Likewise, all caps or any other alternate spelling, none of those will work.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=62)** Only fully lowercase.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=64)** One quick note on terminology.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=66)** Boolean is three syllables and maybe for this reason, booleans are often also called flags.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=72)** So instead of saying, I have a boolean called is enabled, I might say, I have a flag called is enabled.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=80)** So if you hear the term flag, it just means a boolean.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=84)** I can set variables to true or false and by doing so, I would say that this variable is a boolean.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=90)** So I could create a variable called button has been clicked, and set it to false.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=100)** This might track whether someone has clicked a particular button on a page and started out as set to false and then sometime later, when the button actually is clicked, set it to true and act on that information somehow.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=113)** Booleans are important because they're your way of getting answers from your programs about the state of your program's world.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=119)** What I mean is you can ask questions in the form of is this true or is it false?
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=125)** It's one of the questions that JavaScript can answer for you without you having to do a bunch of extra work.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=129)** Questions like this, where you ask something in code, get back the answers are very often in the form of comparisons.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=136)** We'll be looking at those in more detail a little later, but just to give you a preview, it'll be something like this.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=141)** I'll make two variables.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=144)** And I'll say, my first variable is my location.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=148)** I'll set this to Santa Barbara.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=152)** And then I'll set my other location to Los Angeles, and now I can ask a question of JavaScript.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=162)** Are these two variables the same?
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=164)** And the way that you ask that question is by writing a statement, and adding three equal signs.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=172)** This will tell me, are these two things exactly the same?
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=176)** So I start to type my other location and I can use the right arrow to complete it without having to type the whole thing.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=182)** And of course, JavaScript returns false.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=185)** It knows the values of the two variables and it can tell me whether they are the same.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=190)** I defined them right next to each other myself, so this is not all that exciting really.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=195)** But if there's a lot of other code in between the definition and the comparison where the values might change at some point, this could be a very useful question to get an answer to.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=204)** So now if I change my other location to Santa Barbara, like that, and I ask the same question again, and in this case, instead of retyping it, I'll use the up arrow a couple of times to bring back that code that I wrote previously, I can hit return and I get back true.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=225)** We're going to look at comparisons later.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=227)** But one thing to note for now, variables are assigned with one equal sign.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=232)** Comparisons use more than one.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=234)** This is a very popular typo that introduces fun little bugs for every programmer, regardless of level of experience.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=242)** So that's a look at booleans, true and false.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/booleans-for-what-is-true?u=76281980&t=245)** Very useful for answering questions in your programs.

#### Variable mutability
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before we move on from variables, I want to take a moment to look at other ways that variables can be declared.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=7)** The variables that we've seen so far have been changeable or mutable as it's hugely described in programming.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=13)** Declaring a variable with VAR yields a variable whose value can be changed later.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=18)** But there are times in our code where we'd like to have variables that can be set once, but never changed later.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=24)** If we want to represent facts, like the birthdays of family members or how many items are in a dozen, you wouldn't want those facts to be changed later accidentally.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=33)** In situations like this, we want to create constants.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=36)** A constant is an immutable variable.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=39)** In modern JavaScript, the keyword const can do this.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=44)** If I'm going to write some code to simulate a bakery, I could set a few variables for common quantities like dozen, half dozen and bakers dozen.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=57)** Creating these constants, I can use them just like any other variable, which can make my code more readable than having to type six, 12, and 13 all over the place.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=66)** So I can see that here's dozen and then here's half dozen and bakers dozen.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=76)** And most importantly, if I try to change the value of any of these constants, I get an error.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=81)** So let's say I wanted to set dozen to baker's dozen or 13.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=88)** I get an error.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=90)** Errors like this can help programmers avoid mistakes.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=93)** We always prefer our programs to yield an error than to just continue quietly on with hidden mistakes that could bite us later on.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=100)** Along with VAR, I can create mutable, that is, changeable variables using the keyword let.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=107)** So at a basic level, the following are equivalent.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=111)** If I'm going to create a variable called cookie count and set this to five, this would work, but also let would work.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=121)** Doing this, I can change cookie count to something else and it works just fine.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=128)** Cookie count is now six.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=131)** To this point, we've seen three keywords that can be used to create variables, VAR, let and const.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/variable-mutability?u=76281980&t=139)** There are some subtleties beyond mutability that we'll talk about later in a discussion on scope, but as we're continuing to build our vocabulary, this is a good place to leave off.

> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=81)** So I'll paste this into the Terminal and I do, in fact, want to paste all this.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=85)** There we go.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=87)** Great. So now I can see everything over here.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=92)** And now here's my second animal, a bear.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=95)** All the key names in this bear object are the same but they store different data.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=100)** This is something you can expect to do often where you create objects that have a similar structure but different data contained inside.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=107)** With this idea, you're going to start to edge into the territory of object-oriented programming, but we'll talk about that just a little bit later.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=115)** These animal objects map onto things in the real world but you can make objects of anything you need, concrete or abstract.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=123)** Down here, I've made a sort of book of knowledge.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=126)** I can refer to it to find out things like, when lunchtime is, what the ultimate answer to life, the universe and everything is, an idea of the best song and what earth might be considered.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=138)** As you can see, what you actually do with objects is going to vary a lot depending on the kind of program that you're writing.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/using-objects-for-data-modeling?u=76281980&t=144)** But objects are a very powerful way, despite their simplicity, of representing data and making sure that the computer understands the things that you need it to.

#### Jargon: References
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When working with JavaScript objects, there's an important concept called references.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=5)** Objects are references to particular locations in the computer's memory and that impacts your ability to work with them in different contexts.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=13)** So let's create an animal object one more time and check its value.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=19)** I'm going to copy everything from the start of this file into Node, and now when I check the value, I can see everything looks right.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=33)** Now I'm going to create another variable called animal2 and assign animal to it.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=42)** If I inspect animal2, it's all the same stuff.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=47)** Now the question here is, what have I actually done when I executed that assignment statement?
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=52)** Did I make a copy of the animal object?
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=54)** As we will see the answer turns out to be no.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=57)** To demonstrate this, let's change animal2's genus to ursus, a bear.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=64)** So now when I inspect animal2, I see that the genus has been set to ursus, which is what I expect, but if I check the original animal, it has also been changed.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=76)** What we've exposed here is references.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=79)** That assignment statement did not actually make a copy of the object.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=83)** This was actually saying, assign this animal2 location in memory to the same location in memory as the original variable.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=91)** So these two variables reference the same memory and really are the same thing.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=97)** This is an important point that affects all types of JavaScript objects, both the ones that we're looking at now and others that are based on objects internally, like arrays, maps, and sets.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=109)** That assignment does not make a copy of the object.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=112)** It just gives us two variables that point to the same value, the exact same value.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=117)** So if I want animal2 to be a copy of all the same stuff, one way to do that is to execute the whole original data blob a second time, but assign it to animal2 this time.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=131)** So if I copy and paste all this and now I check animal2.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=138)** Right here, I can see that it's genus is corvis, and animal one is still set to ursus.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=143)** As an aside, creating a complete accurate copy of an object is a little bit tricky in JavaScript.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=149)** There are a few ways that you might see this done in the wild, and I have a few a little further down in this file.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=157)** The first is using the assigned method on the global object Object.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=163)** As used here, we're copying every property from the original animal object into an empty object and assigning that new copy to animal2.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=175)** As with most things in programming, there are subtleties that you'll run into if you work with JavaScript for a while, but that's a good start.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=182)** You might see the same line written like this, with the three dots.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=187)** This is called the spread operator.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=189)** Those three dots are called the spread operator in ECMAScript 2015, and it basically means, spread out all the data from this object, in this case into a new copy.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=200)** These two lines are basically equivalent.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=203)** These three dots are used in other ways in JavaScript as well, which is a bit confusing at first, but you'll get used to it with time, experience, and probably many trips to your favorite search engine.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=213)** Finally, there's this method which uses the built-in JSON object and we're taking that animal object, turning it into a JSON representation that's a string, and then parsing it again into a new object from that original JSON.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=232)** JSON, if you haven't heard of it before, stands for JavaScript Object Notation and it's a way to represent data in a way that looks like JavaScript, but is very portable and represented as plain text.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=244)** If your object is made of data that can be represented as JSON, this is a good method to use and you'll likely see it in code out in the world.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=252)** Anyway, once that type of copying is done, I can change animal2 however I want and animal one will not be affected.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=260)** The takeaway here is to keep references in mind when you're working with objects.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=265)** When you assign an existing object to a new variable, you're not making a copy of it.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=269)** You're reusing a reference to the same value, the same location in the computer's memory.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-references?u=76281980&t=274)** If you want a copy, you need to do a little extra work to make one.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), is an  (1), is called (1), basically means (1), stands for (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), this, (2), while, (1), finally, (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (4), node (1)
> **Env Vars:** json (5)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Arrays
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video we're going to talk about arrays which are a way of storing lists of data that preserve their order.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=6)** We've already looked at objects which let you group data together, but there are some cases where you want to store say a list of things, and the order of those things actually matters.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=17)** For example, let's say you want it to store a list of the days of the week, which of course appear in a certain order, or the months of the year, something like that.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=26)** In cases like those, you'll want to use an array.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=29)** So how do we make one?
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=31)** Let's create a variable and call it myArray.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=34)** And instead of using curly braces, as we did with objects, we use square braces like this.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=40)** That is the form of an array literal.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=46)** And so now if I check the value of my array, I get back those same empty square braces.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=53)** If I wanted to create a list of days of the week, I'll just add those two square braces again and now I'll fill it up with some of the days of the week.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=65)** I'll just add a few in here.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=67)** Certainly don't need to do everything.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=74)** As you can see, unlike with an object where the order of the keys isn't guaranteed, an array does preserve the order that you put things in.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=82)** And there are no keys here, only the values.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=85)** Strictly speaking, that's not true.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=86)** There are keys, they're just not being displayed because this is an array.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=91)** Under the hood, an array in JavaScript is actually an object that gets special treatment.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=97)** This is not the case in every programming language.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=99)** In many others, there are objects and there are arrays, and they're totally different.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=105)** In JavaScript, arrays are based on objects internally but arrays do have a few special qualities.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=112)** One is that the order is preserved and another is that the keys are automatically assigned, and they're assigned as numbers starting with zero.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=122)** So even if I don't care about the order, I can fill an array with a list of stuff and just not have to think about naming a key for every stored value.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=129)** And again, you can create arrays of any kind of data.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=133)** Let's create a new one, call it myList, and fill it up with some things.

> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=189)** Arrays are often used for lists, so if, for example, you wanted to store the counties for some area because you're making a program that needs some kind of geographic knowledge, arrays can be a good fit.

> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=213)** In the case of something like this, the order might not for the purposes of your program, make any difference, but if it did make a difference, you'd have already guaranteed that they'd appear in this order.

> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=237)** And so here's the thing.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=239)** Arrays can contain objects.

> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=241)** So I made an object here as the first item.

>

>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=259)** The point here is that you can store anything in arrays the same way you can with objects.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=264)** Arrays in JavaScript are really just specially treated objects after all.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=268)** The main limits are your needs and your imagination.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=271)** There's one more thing I want to point out about arrays that's different from regular objects which is that an array knows how many items are in it.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=279)** Just as strings know how long they are, an array also knows how long it is.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=285)** So if I want to see how many things are in my list of stuff, I can use the dot notation and get its length property.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=293)** All arrays have that length property.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=296)** I can see this one is four items long.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=298)** In other words, it has four items inside it.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=301)** That doesn't account for anything that's nested, it's only how many objects are at the root level of this array of stuff.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=309)** There will be some occasions in your programming where you're going to want to use arrays and there will be some times where you'll want to use objects and you'll want to combine them in various interesting ways.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=318)** It all depends on the requirements of whatever you're writing at the time.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=322)** Right now we're just going through syntax so you can know some of the tools that are available to you in the JavaScript toolbox.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arrays?u=76281980&t=328)** And that was a look at arrays, which can store lists of anything you want preserving the order that you put them in.

> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/manipulating-arrays?u=76281980&t=400)** So if we want to get rid of this undefined empty item, I'll put in that same index of two.

> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/manipulating-arrays?u=76281980&t=407)** Then a comma, and the second parameter is how many items I want to get rid of.
>
> **[6:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/manipulating-arrays?u=76281980&t=413)** So in this case I'll use one 'cause I just want to get rid of one item and it returns an array containing that one item that was removed.
>
> **[7:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/manipulating-arrays?u=76281980&t=423)** So now let's take a look at that counties array again, and there we go.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/manipulating-arrays?u=76281980&t=427)** We've actually cut the array down so it's length, the number of items in it, is what we expect.
>
> **[7:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/manipulating-arrays?u=76281980&t=433)** This is an important thing to remember with arrays.
>
> **[7:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/manipulating-arrays?u=76281980&t=436)** The length always refers to the number of slots in the array, not whether the slots have something meaningful in them.
>
> **[7:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/manipulating-arrays?u=76281980&t=443)** For more on arrays, you can check out the Mozilla JavaScript documentation for a full list of what's available.
>
> **[7:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/manipulating-arrays?u=76281980&t=449)** But you've now had a taste of how to manipulate arrays, how to read what's in them, add to, and remove from them.

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-whitespace?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-whitespace?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to talk about how to make your code more readable through the use of whitespace.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-whitespace?u=76281980&t=5)** In other words, spaces, returns, and so forth.

>

>

>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-whitespace?u=76281980&t=116)** No spaces can sneak in.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-whitespace?u=76281980&t=118)** As you can see, the built-in code checker gets very angry when I have that extra space in there.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-whitespace?u=76281980&t=126)** Now, the way you set up your whitespace is something that programmers will, for lack of a better word, fight over.
>

>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-whitespace?u=76281980&t=153)** I've become a huge fan of Prettier and use it all the time in my JavaScript projects.

>

> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-whitespace?u=76281980&t=172)** That doesn't happen on this particular file because there's a configuration dotfile, this prettierignore file down here, that lists file names that are not to be included in Prettier's processing.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-whitespace?u=76281980&t=187)** In any case, when you're starting out, you might just be working on your own projects for yourself and the main issue is to keep your code readable for yourself.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-whitespace?u=76281980&t=195)** However carefully you want to do it, I recommend embracing the use of whitespace to make sure that your code is readable without affecting how it's executed.
