#### Readability: Comments
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to talk about comments which are used for documenting and for disabling execution of parts of your code.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=7)** The most important function for comments is documentation, that is, adding pieces of code that don't execute, but can be read by other human beings or by yourself to understand what's going on in your program.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=20)** These come in two styles in JavaScript, line comments and block comments.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=25)** The first is the line comment.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=27)** We can see that on the first line of this file, it starts with two slash characters and anything after those on the same line doesn't execute.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=36)** These need to be immediately following one another.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=38)** No spaces in between or anything like that.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=40)** Just two slashes.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=42)** And they can be at the beginning of the line or anywhere else.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=45)** So in this case, I have one that's at the beginning of the line, and then this one down here is at the end of the line.
>

>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=55)** It's pretty clear what this assignment does, but at least you can see that it works.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=61)** The other style is block comments.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=63)** You can see that right here.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=65)** This starts with a slash followed by an asterisk with no space in between, and then you can write as much as you want across multiple lines, and it finally needs to end with an asterisk followed by a slash.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=79)** You'll see this all the time in JavaScript for inserting long paragraphs or just other elements where a lot of text is needed in one place, and maybe it would get a little bit tedious to add those double slashes in front of every single line.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=91)** My text editor here makes it really obvious what's contained in the comment thanks to syntax highlighting.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=97)** If I remove this ending asterisk, and scroll down a little bit, you can see that the entire rest of the file ends up becoming a comment, almost.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=105)** You can see a little semicolon down here, which we'll get to in a second.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=110)** But block comments are really useful and you'll see them all the time.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=113)** You just need to be careful with these, because, every once in a while, there'll be something like this, which is not actually a comment piece, it's part of a regular expression, but because it uses the same characters if I've forgotten the ending token for that block comment, this will be interpreted as a comment.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=136)** But thanks to syntax highlighting, I can see what's going on here and I can add that asterisk and slash back in to restore order to this file.
>

>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/readability-comments?u=76281980&t=155)** The other function of a JavaScript comment is just to disable parts.

#### Regular expressions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this lesson, we're going to take a look at regular expressions, which are a way of searching text for patterns.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=6)** When you're working with text like strings and JavaScript, but also if you're working with long runs of text in prose, there may be times when you want to find a particular something, but maybe not an exact something.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=19)** Maybe you can describe it with a pattern that it should follow, like two digits followed by any number of letters, or any word that starts with A and ends with S.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=28)** A regular expression is a way to describe something that you're searching for with an abstract pattern.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=33)** In JavaScript, a regular expression is another data type just like numbers or strings, and it looks like this.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=41)** Now, if we just have two slashes with nothing in between 'em, that is, of course, a line comment, but it becomes a regular expression when there's something in between those slashes.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=52)** That can be words, it can be numbers, it can be various combinations, and it can even be patterns.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=59)** We're going to look at some simple examples in this lesson, but entire books have been written on regular expressions.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=65)** This particular one called "Mastering Regular Expressions" is the one that I've read myself.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=71)** Regular expressions are tremendously powerful and usable in all good text editors and word processors.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=77)** If you spend much time writing code or, indeed, any kind of writing, learning the basics of regular expressions is really worthwhile.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=84)** I don't want to go too overboard and say they're life-changing, but it's honestly pretty close.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=89)** Anyway, here's how they work.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=92)** What we're going to do here is take these four strings, which have some different stuff in them, as you can see, similar but different, paste them in here, so they're all available in Node.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=106)** And then I have this regular expression.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=108)** It's a variable called regex, and it's just very simple with the word this in between those two slashes.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=115)** What we're going to do is we're going to take this regex and we're going to use its test method.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=123)** And so I can copy and paste any of these and try them out.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=131)** In fact, let's take all four of them.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=134)** We'll execute all of them together.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=140)** So we can see the result of each test.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=143)** When we checked it against the first string, the result was false.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=147)** It did not find this.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=148)** Likewise, the second one, the only one where it returned true was the third string.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=153)** And I can see that the word this does appear here, but it's not in the same case.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=158)** This can be useful in a lot of different ways.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=161)** You can check for a word or a phrase or whatever in a block of text of any length and that test happens really fast.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=169)** There are a lot of modifiers that you can add to regular expressions.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=173)** We're going to try a few variations down here below.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=177)** First, we're we're going to try adding the i flag onto the end to modify that regular expression.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=186)** Flags like this that you add on the end, it's like a boolean, but this enables a feature, which, in this case, means make this regular expression case insensitive.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=196)** So now I can run these tests again.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=200)** And this time with the regular expression set to be case insensitive, string four, which was previously false, comes back true.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=209)** And that should be the case for any of these others as well.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=214)** Now they all come back true.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=217)** There are some other basic modifiers that we can add to the regular expression as well.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=223)** A carrot will match against the beginning of a string.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=228)** So if I modify this to match against the beginning, and then run these four tests again, now string one, two, and four come back true and three comes back false, because this appears at the beginning of each of these strings and in the middle of string three.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=254)** I can also check for whether this appears at the end by following what I'm matching with a dollar sign.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=267)** So now we can try this same test against all four strings.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=272)** And because this does not appear at the end of any of them, it comes back false for each one.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=280)** Just a couple more here.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=284)** In this one, we're trying to test if ever appears at the end.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=290)** And I would expect this to come back true for two of these.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=295)** So we'll modify our expression and run these tests.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=301)** One and two come back true, and then three and four come back false.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=308)** But there's something tricky about that particular regular expression we just tried, because a dot has a special meaning in a regular expression.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=315)** It doesn't check for a literal period or full stop.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=319)** This means any character.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=321)** So if I want to check for a literal dot, I would have to escape it like this.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=327)** But there are lots of different special characters that can appear in regular expressions to do different kinds of matching.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=334)** We've only begun to scratch the surface of what regular expressions can do.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=338)** Along with checking for matches, as we've done here, you can extract chunks of matching text for further transformations, search and replace by patterns, instead of by literal strings, and tons more.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=349)** This topic has applications way beyond JavaScript.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=353)** Regular expressions are extremely powerful and widely available.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=356)** If you find yourself wanting to process any kind of text, regular expressions can come to the rescue most of the time.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=362)** I know when I first learned how to use them, it felt like the text world was my oyster, and I hope they're helpful to you as well.

### 5. 4. Operators and Control Structures

#### Simple comparisons
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to look at simple comparisons, which are the beginning of the way that we can ask the computer a question about something and get back a meaningful answer.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=10)** Going to maximize the terminal here to give us as much room as we can possibly have.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=15)** The first one we'll look at is equality.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=18)** That is knowing whether something is the same as something else.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=22)** We'll start out really easy with this and I'll create a couple of variables here.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=27)** We'll create a variable called one, which will contain the number one and another variable called two, which will contain the number two.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=35)** We can just type these out to make sure that they have the values we expect.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=39)** Most of the time at least one of the items will be asking a question about will be a variable.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=43)** So having these two available is helpful.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=47)** First, we're going to ask if one and one are the same.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=52)** The way I'm going to do that is using an operator made of three equal signs, one after another, 1, 2, 3.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=58)** This operator is called the strict equality operator and it tests whether the object on the left is identical to the one on the right.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=67)** And as we can see here, the answer is true.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=71)** There's a similar operator that asks whether this item on the left is not identical to the one on the right, that's called strict inequality.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=82)** And to do that, we just substitute that first equal sign for an exclamation mark, which programmers also call a bang.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=88)** So in this case we're asking is one not identical to one?
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=92)** We would expect the answer to be false, and indeed it is.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=98)** Now, if we change that second one to our variable two, and because we're asking here are these two things strictly not the same, we get back the answer true.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=110)** And then finally we'll change that identical, that not identical back to the strict equality operator again.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=119)** And our answer is false.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=123)** So at this point we've started to ask some questions and started to get back some answers.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=128)** These kind of comparisons are all over the place in most programs.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=132)** You might be asking, is this page's URL about dot HTML or is this button that was just clicked the main menu button?
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=139)** Checking whether something is equal to something else is a comparison you'll just be doing a lot.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=145)** There's another pair of operators that are similar, but act differently.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=149)** You'll see them a lot in programs out in the wild.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=152)** These are simply the equality and inequality operators with only two equal signs or one bang and one equal sign.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=159)** These also check equality, but in a way that's not as strict hence the difference in names.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=164)** So let's try them out.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=166)** First, we'll do a regular equality check on the variables one with itself and that still comes back as true because they are the same.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=176)** After this, things get a little weird.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=180)** So let's try one.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=183)** And for the thing that we will compare it to let's create a string with the number one in the string.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=190)** So it's a string, but it has one inside it.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=195)** And in terms of this kind of not strict equality, the answer is true.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=202)** Now, strictly they are different, but this operator sort of implicitly converts the data types from string to number in this case and says, "Yeah, sure, they're pretty much the same."
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=212)** The inequality operator does the same thing for inequality, so we get the opposite of the previous weird result.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=222)** It is generally recommended that you use the strict versions of these operators for the most predictable results in your code, but people do still often use the non-strict versions as well.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=232)** The particulars of which kinds of values will return true or false for the equality and inequality operator are a little bit too detailed to go into here, especially for people who are just starting out.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=243)** You can look at the equality operators section of the operators page on the Mozilla developer network for more information on this.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=251)** JavaScript also has relational operators, which are straight out of your math class.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=256)** So for example, we could ask is one less than two?
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=262)** Of course it is.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=263)** And so that returns true.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=266)** Then we can ask is one greater than two just by flipping that angle bracket.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=272)** And indeed that's false.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=273)** We can also combine greater or less than with an equal sign.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=280)** Less than or equal to is the same angle bracket as we saw above directly followed by an equal sign.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=287)** So now we're asking is one less than or equal to two?
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=292)** And indeed it is.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=293)** So that returns true.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=297)** Likewise, we can ask is one less than or equal to one?
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=301)** The answer is true because indeed one is equal to one.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=306)** Of course we can ask is one greater than or equal to two?
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=313)** The answer of course is no.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=314)** So that returns false.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=316)** And of course we don't have to use variables.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=319)** Our final question will be is 10 greater than or equal to two, just the number 10.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=326)** And of course that is true.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=329)** So that concludes our first look at comparisons, the various equality operators, and the relational operators.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/simple-comparisons?u=76281980&t=335)** Expect to use all of these early and often.

#### Arithmetic operators
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to look at some simple operators that are used for doing math.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=5)** You're not going to need a lot of math in the examples we're going through in this course, but simple arithmetic does come up pretty often.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=11)** The first four operators are pretty simple and if you've ever used a calculator program before, these will probably be familiar to you.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=19)** We have of course, the plus operator which takes two numbers and adds them together.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=23)** So two plus five equals seven.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=28)** We also have minus which is actually a hyphen, so four minus three is one.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=34)** Of course, we're not restricted to the counting numbers.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=37)** We can do negative numbers as well.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=39)** So five minus nine equals negative four.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=43)** To do multiplication, we don't use an x or a dot.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=46)** We use an asterisk.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=47)** So three asterisk five is three times five which is 15.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=55)** And then finally, we use a slash, also known as a forward slash, to do division.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=60)** So 36 divided by six is six.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=65)** And when you start getting into fractions, the result is expressed using a decimal point.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=70)** So we can divide 36 by five, we get 7.2.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=76)** So far these are all pretty straightforward.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=78)** Probably nothing you haven't seen before, but now we're going to look at a few things that might be a little unusual depending on the sort of math you've been exposed to before.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=86)** The first one is the modulus operator which is a percent sign.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=90)** That modulus operator takes the first operand, divides it by the second, and then returns the remainder of that division.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=98)** This is often used for determining programmatically whether a number is even or odd, but you could also use it for something like if I'm showing a list of search results in groups of 20.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=107)** How many will be on that last page of results?
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=110)** Anyway here's what it looks like in usage.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=113)** So we'll start with 20 modulus or you might just say mod two.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=120)** 20 divided by two is 10 and there's no remainder, so we expect this to return zero and indeed it does.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=128)** 19 is of course, an odd number.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=131)** 19 divided by two is nine with a remainder of one.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=137)** If we wanted to find out if a number was even, we'd do a comparison that looks like this.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=142)** 20 mod two, and then use our strict equality operator to ask is this equal to zero?
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=151)** Normally we'd probably be using a variable somewhere in here instead of just two scaler values.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=156)** That's what it's called when we're using a value directly instead of assigning it to a variable first, but this makes it very clear what we're checking.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=165)** Now let's answer that how many search results are on the last page of this query question?
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=171)** I'll create a couple of fake variables for how many items are on each page and another one to fake how many results we have.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=178)** So we'll say per page is going to be 20 and we'll make another one for the total results.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=187)** Let's say that's 254 results.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=190)** So now I want to find out the remainder of total results.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=196)** I'm going to need this to be capitalized properly.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=199)** So I want the remainder of total results when divided by the number of pages.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=206)** So that's going to be 14 because 20 times 20 is 240, plus 14 is 254.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=216)** Now let's look at incrementing and decrementing which is something you do all the time in programming.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=221)** Let's say I have a variable called counter which starts out at zero.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=227)** Many times when you're working through something in programming, you need to keep track of how often you've done that thing.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=232)** Every time you do whatever that unit of work is, you tell your counter all right, I've done this one more time so increment yourself, and there are various ways that we can do that.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=241)** The first one is to take counter and assign it to itself plus one.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=251)** This does work, counter is now one, but it's a little verbose.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=257)** There's always a tension in programming between keeping things verbose enough that they're easy to read and not having to type a lot for operations that are very simple.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=265)** So there are various ways to express the same thing that are just a little shorter.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=269)** The first one is to take your variable and then use the plus-equals, also known as the addition assignment operator.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=276)** So in this case, if I say counter plus equals one, this is expressing exactly the same thing as the previous statement but it's just a little shorter.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=286)** Still pretty clear what it's doing though.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=288)** Another common way is to take the variable name and simply add two plus signs on the end of it.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=295)** Now notice that the return value here was still two.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=299)** But if I check the value of counter, it actually was incremented.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=303)** Another thing to watch out for is that this will only work incrementing by one.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=308)** Whereas with plus-equals, you can increment by any arbitrary number.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=312)** So I could say counter plus equals five if I wanted to.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=317)** I can even use a negative number.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=319)** So I could add to my count negative four, and that still works.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=327)** You can use whatever feels right to you and similar to incrementing, there's also decrementing operators.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=332)** They're exactly the same thing except instead of using a plus sign, you use minus.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=338)** So if I want to count down from somewhere, I can say counter minus-equals one.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=346)** Likewise there is a variation with two minus signs.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=351)** Again, the value returned is three, but the actual value in the variable ends up being two.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=358)** One thing that's worth mentioning is that all of the arithmetic operators we've seen so far work with this assignment syntax.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=365)** So for example, counter asterisk equals two will double the value in counter.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=376)** I don't see this construct very often, but if you ever do, that's what it means.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=381)** One more note about these arithmetic operators.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=384)** The type of data you use them on can yield different results.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=387)** This is particularly important in a language like JavaScript that doesn't care what data you put in the variables.
>
> **[6:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=393)** Plus also works on strings.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=395)** But when you use it with strings, it's called a concatenation operator.
>
> **[6:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=399)** So if I say cat plus dog, I get back catdog.
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=406)** They're just stuck together.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=408)** If you ever want to combine strings as words, you need to make sure that spaces are included in there somewhere.
>
> **[6:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=413)** So I can add a space to that first cat string and now the result is cat space dog.
>
> **[7:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=421)** And you aren't limited to two strings.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=425)** You can do this as many times as you want.
>
> **[7:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=426)** So cat and dog, and we get back a little phrase, cat and dog.
>
> **[7:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=437)** So that plus operator works on strings to concatenate them together.
>
> **[7:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=441)** While working in arithmetic terms, it's just simple addition.
>
> **[7:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=445)** In the previous video, we saw how numbers containing strings can sometimes act as if they contain numbers instead of strings, and here's a little trap you want to avoid.
>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=454)** Say we have two strings with one and two inside and I use that plus operator on them.
>
> **[7:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=464)** The result is a string containing those two digits squashed together, not the numbers added together.
>
> **[7:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=470)** So this is a string with 12, not three.
>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=475)** It's concatenation, not addition.
>
> **[7:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=477)** I think that it might be an unofficial rite of passage for all beginning developers to do this accidentally at least once, myself surely included.
>
> **[8:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arithmetic-operators?u=76281980&t=485)** That is an overview of the most common and probably most useful arithmetic operators and what they do, with a short detour on string concatenation.

#### Logical operators
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to introduce some of JavaScript's logical operators.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=4)** There are many, but we'll look at the three most common, the logical and, or, and not, also known as conjunction, disjunction, and negation, if you're fancy.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=16)** When working through a JavaScript program, there will be many times when you want to do comparisons on more than one thing at a time, basically asking a question in multiple parts.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=25)** Logical operators let us combine these parts in structured ways.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=29)** We'll start with and and or.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=33)** So as you can see, in JavaScript, those look like two ampersands for and and two pipe characters for or, which on an American keyboard you can find above the return key, on the backslash key.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=47)** You hold the shift key, type a backslash, and you get one of these pipes.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=52)** That's what they look like, but how do we use them?
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=55)** I'm going to paste in a one-liner here, setting three variables.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=60)** animal1 is a monkey, animal2 is a bear, and animal3 is a tiger.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=65)** After these are established in our program, let's assume that there's other code that might change what's in each variable.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=72)** Later on in the program, we want to check what's actually in the variables at that point.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=78)** I can use the strict equality operator to ask in JavaScript, is animal1 still a monkey?
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=86)** And maybe I want to ask at the same time, is animal2 still a bear?
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=93)** If it's important to check them both, and then execute a piece of code only if both of those are true at the same time, I can use the logical and right here in the middle to check them both simultaneously.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=108)** So here, I'm asking, is animal1 a monkey, and is animal2 a bear?
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=113)** And JavaScript says, "Yes, this is true, because the whole statement evaluates down to true."
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=122)** Now, if I wanted to find out if animal1 is an ape and animal2 is a bear, we'll change that first one to ape.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=133)** And JavaScript returns false here.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=136)** The idea is that the and operator will return true only if every condition that you pass into it, separated by that and, is true.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=146)** This is the case no matter how many comparisons I add on.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=149)** So let's check animal3 as well.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=153)** If any one of these conditions is false, the entire statement evaluates down to false.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=160)** But then if I go back to the beginning, and I change ape to monkey, all three are true, so the entire statement with all those ands evaluates to true.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=175)** If I only care about one of these conditions, I can use or, instead.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=180)** I can ask, is animal1 a monkey, or, using the two pipes, is animal2 a bear?
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=187)** And that is true.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=189)** So once again, I'm going to change that first monkey to ape.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=194)** And now I'm asking, is animal1 an ape, or is animal2 a bear?
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=199)** If either one of them is true, the final answer for the whole statement will be true.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=205)** For an or to return false, everything needs to be false.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=208)** So let me change bear to ostrich.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=212)** And now, because neither of these is true, in other words, they both are false, the entire statement is false.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=221)** You can also combine these ands and ors on a single line.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=225)** But now things get a little more complicated.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=227)** JavaScript has a notion of operator precedence, which determines which of these things will be evaluated first, and then collapsed into its true or false value before being compared against the next thing.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=240)** And in JavaScript, with operator precedence, the ands are always evaluated down before the ors.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=247)** You can also combine these ands and ors on a single line.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=252)** But now things get a little more complicated.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=254)** JavaScript has a notion of operator precedence, which determines which of these things will be evaluated first, and then collapsing those true and false values before comparing them to the next statement.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=266)** In JavaScript, with operator precedence, the ands are always evaluated before the ors.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=274)** So in this case, because these two are joined by an and, they would be evaluated first before being joined with an or to this first statement.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=285)** So animal3 is a tiger, but animal2 is not a monkey.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=290)** So this would evaluate down to false.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=293)** And then animal1, it is a monkey, so this would be true.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=298)** Therefore, we have true or false, and the whole statement evaluates to true.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=305)** So if I have a series of comparisons like this, and I want to make sure that the ors are kept together and evaluated first, I can add parentheses.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=315)** And now these two are evaluated first, which would be true, and then checked against animal3 equals tiger with that and.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=324)** And the result is again true here.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=327)** Now, I know at a certain point your eyes could be rolling into the back of your head from seeing true and false all the time, but there will be times in writing your code, or in reading other folks' code, where you're going to see lots of comparisons.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=338)** And hopefully they will have made things easier for you by putting in parentheses where needed and generally trying to make things as clear as possible.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=347)** Let's talk about one more thing with logical operators, negation, or not.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=353)** This is written as an exclamation mark, also called the bang, to invert from true to false, or the other way around.
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=361)** So here's how it works.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=363)** If I write not true, or bang true, the result is false.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=368)** Likewise, not false returns true.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=373)** You can apply not to entire comparisons as well, wrapping the whole thing in parentheses and sticking a not in front of it.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=381)** So let's take one of these from above.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=384)** I'll copy this.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=387)** I'll make animal2 a zebra.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=392)** So this is false, but then if I put a not in front of it, wrap the whole thing in parentheses, it becomes true.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=400)** As an aside, sometimes you might want to take an expression that's entirely negated this way and write it out without parentheses for reasons of readability or maybe clarity of intent.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=410)** When converting an expression like this one, the not isn't just altering these strict equality operators.
>
> **[6:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=417)** It also flips any ands to ors and vice versa.
>
> **[7:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=421)** And and or are logical opposites.
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=424)** So if I wanted to rewrite this without the negation, and I only changed the equality operators, this evaluates to false.
>
> **[7:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=434)** So this is not equivalent to the line before.
>
> **[7:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=438)** All of these have to be flipped to their opposite.
>
> **[7:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=441)** So that and becomes an or, and now it evaluates to true.
>
> **[7:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=447)** Logical operators are rarely used in standalone statements like this.
>
> **[7:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=450)** In the real world, they're almost always used in control structures, which determine what parts of a program will be executed and which ones will not.
>
> **[7:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=458)** Control structures like conditional statements and loops are coming up next.
>
> **[7:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=461)** And you use these and other comparisons we've looked at in this chapter.
>
> **[7:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/logical-operators?u=76281980&t=466)** Now that you've seen several kinds of operators that can make comparisons and answer questions about the state of a program, we're ready to move on to conditionals.

> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=406)** Then I'll only need to check specifically for YES or NO with specific responses to each of those.
>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=411)** Anything else, whatever the person types, would go into the else clause.
>
> **[6:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=415)** And you can have as many of these lines as you want.
>
> **[6:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=419)** And the conditions in these parentheses don't have to be single comparisons.
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=424)** You can also add logical operators.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=427)** As in this case, we are checking if answer equals YES or answer equals NO where we might take some common actions for YES or NO.
>
> **[7:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=438)** Anything that appears in those parentheses is the condition under which this whole block in these curly braces that match here will execute.
>
> **[7:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=447)** You can also nest if blocks inside other ones.
>
> **[7:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=451)** So in these curly braces, I have another block of if and else.
>
> **[7:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=458)** We check the same is answer YES and then have an else here.
>
> **[7:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=463)** So this allows you to do common actions that would match for either of these, because we're using an OR, and then get into specifics inside that clause.
>
> **[7:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=473)** You can see here that there's a lot of power in being able to branch off and do different things in your code based on different inputs or different other conditions, whether it's input from the user or conditions that you created somewhere in your own code.
>
> **[8:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-if?u=76281980&t=488)** And that is how you can use if statements to execute different code under different conditions.

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to take a look at another way of doing conditional branching in JavaScript: the switch statement.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=7)** It's similar to if and else statements in results, but it looks different and acts differently at times.
>

>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=82)** Then there's whatever is supposed to happen just like in an if block, but there are no surrounding curly braces.

> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=89)** And at the end of each case, you have the break keyword.

> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=109)** And then the first case we're looking at is yes, in other words, is answer equal to yes.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=114)** If that is true, this block will execute.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=118)** If not, we continue down to the next one, which is maybe, then to no, and finally to one called default which is the same as an else statement.

> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=212)** There's one thing that's a little bit tricky with switch statements that you need to be aware of.

>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=250)** But then I also get You said maybe, so this is the thing about switch statements.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=256)** If you don't include a break, the execution will cascade into subsequent case blocks until a break is reached.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=265)** So when I typed in yes, that first block got executed, but there's no break here so it continued into the next case.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=274)** You want to be sure that if you want just a single block to execute that you include that break statement, but you can also leave the break off on purpose if you want a certain block of code to be able to cascade to certain other cases.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=288)** This can be a little bit tough to do with if statements.

>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/conditionals-switch?u=76281980&t=305)** You can use either one. It's really up to you.

> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=133)** Here's a scenario to illustrate that.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=135)** When writing applications, we want to track errors and report them back to the user at some sensible point.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=141)** So in this first case, we'll have a variable called errorMsg and we'll just start out as an empty string.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=149)** If there's an error to report to the user, we can set that string to a message about the error like that field is required or what have you.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=157)** And then later if we find that the error message variable has something in it, we can take some sort of action.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=164)** In the if statements we've seen so far, we have a full expression that then evaluates to being true or false.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=171)** We can just check a single variable or a part of a variable in the same way For this type of evaluation, we're not concerned with an expression evaluating to exactly true or exactly false.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=183)** Here there's a concept of truthy and falsey.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=187)** All values in JavaScript can be considered truthy or falsey for this purpose.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=191)** Generally speaking, if the variable has something in it, it's probably truthy and empty values are falsey.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=198)** There's a page here on the Mozilla JavaScript documentation that gives you a lot of examples of truthy and falsey values if you're curious.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=207)** So if I want to know if there's an error to concern myself with, I can just say if errorMsg which is a lot simpler than if errorMsg.length is greater than zero or something of that nature.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=222)** If the string has anything in it, it will be considered truthy and the error message would be logged.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=228)** So we could say console.error.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=231)** "There was an error," and we can include the error message here, something like that.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=238)** And because errorMsg is empty, it is considered falsey and so that does not execute.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=245)** Negation will take truthy values and make them falsey, just as it will invert true and false.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=252)** So if I rewrite this as if not errorMsg and this time instead of a console.error, I could do console.log, "Yay!"
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=263)** Something simple like just "Yay, no errors!"
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=267)** And that actually does execute because negating this falsey value turns it to truthy and so it'll execute.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=274)** One more example of truthiness.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=276)** Instead of taking a single error message in a string, let's have an array so we can track multiple errors and report them all at once.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=285)** How might you do a simple test of whether there are errors to report before logging them?
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=290)** If you thought if errors would work, it's a totally great guess but it wouldn't work exactly as you expect because an empty array is still a truthy value.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=300)** It's kind of counterintuitive, but that's the truth.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=305)** We need to check the length property of errors instead.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=309)** When there's nothing in there, length will be zero and if there's anything in the array, it will be greater than zero which is truthy.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=318)** So in that case, we'd be able to say console.error, "Please fix these errors," and just do a bit of simple logging there.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=331)** So this would work as expected.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=333)** So be sure to test scenarios or reference documentation when dealing with truthiness and falseness.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=340)** Making if blocks and the conditions that they contain shorter is a very common practice.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=344)** While I don't recommend one-line if statements, you now know of their existence.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-one-liners?u=76281980&t=348)** Employing truthiness in conditions is very common indeed, so feel free to use it but be sure to test the behavior of those conditions as you're coming to grips with it.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **CLI Commands:** make (3), node (1), find (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** errormsg (5)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3)
