#### Terse ifs: Ternaries
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-ternaries?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-ternaries?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we'll look at one more quite terse if construct, which is called the ternary operator or the conditional operator.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-ternaries?u=76281980&t=9)** It's an expression of some kind followed by a question mark, followed by an action to take if that expression evaluates to true, then a colon, and then the other action to take if the expression evaluates to false.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-ternaries?u=76281980&t=26)** So I'm going to start with a variable to give me something to play with here.

>

>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/terse-ifs-ternaries?u=76281980&t=59)** And if it is, we'll log a message to the console that says "You will be a cat herder."

#### Type checking
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] At this point, we're now aware of many data types in JavaScript.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=4)** We know about numbers, strings, booleans, objects, arrays, and so forth.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=11)** There will be times in JavaScript where you want to know what type of data a variable contains.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=16)** We're going to look at that in this lesson.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=19)** JavaScript doesn't particularly care about data types to the same extent that other languages do.

>

>

>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=37)** Going to make a variable called thing and put a number in it.

>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=63)** During the execution of a program, there will be many times when you're dealing with uncertainty.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=69)** You can get a variable in a particular portion of your program and not know necessarily what's in there, but you know what sort of data should be in there.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=78)** Then if the data type is correct, you might follow one branch in your code, and anything else might trigger an error.

> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=84)** In situations like this, you need to check the data type.

>

>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=218)** And our rather verbose type checker returns true.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=222)** If I set thing back to an empty object, I'll use the up arrow a few times to get that code back, and then run the same long type check again, I get false.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=232)** So that entire expression will tell me whether thing is an array or not.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=238)** There are other instances where typeof will not return something that you expect.
>

>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=249)** It's called Not a Number.
>

>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=255)** Number. Call that a little counterintuitive.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=259)** So if we're doing some mathematical work, and we want to see if the result is Not a Number, using typeof is not the way you want to do that.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=268)** There's a built-in Number object, which has a method called is Not a Number.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=274)** And that's what will help you in that case.
>

>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=284)** And if we pass in a regular number, we get false.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=287)** Likewise, we can check the type of null.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=292)** We get object, which is honestly pretty deeply weird.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=295)** So if we want to check whether a variable is null, we would want to use the strict equality operator.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=301)** Let me set thing to null, and then we can use strict equality to check whether thing is null.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=310)** And it is true.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=312)** typeof can also return undefined, in some instances.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=316)** A couple of places where you'll see this is checking the type of a variable that was initialized, but never had any associated data, or when the variable simply doesn't exist.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=327)** So let me create a variable called somethingLater.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=330)** Let's check its type.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=335)** And there it is. Undefined.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=337)** Likewise, if I check the type of a variable that I haven't even created, just call it nothingSilly, still undefined.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=347)** With type checking, there are many subtleties that you'll run into with different special values in JavaScript that won't necessarily give you back the type that you expect.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=356)** So you need to sort of careful with this.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=358)** It's an area where many, myself included, have needed to reference the documentation often.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=364)** Despite that, it can still be useful for basic type checking.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=368)** If you need or want to get more serious about type checking in your JavaScript code, you might consider using helper functions from popular libraries like Lodash.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=376)** If you search the documentation on Lodash for "is," you'll see a whole bunch of helper methods that can be used for checking lots of different kind of objects.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=387)** You could even use TypeScript, which is sort of a version of JavaScript that includes strict type checking.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=392)** But that's well past where we are right now.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/type-checking?u=76281980&t=395)** Just know that basic type checking is included in JavaScript with the typeof operator, and there are options out there for more if you need them.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (9), this, (3), pass (3), case, (2), type. (2)
> **Definitions:** is an  (4), is a  (2), in other words (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** hasownproperty (2), somethinglater (1), nothingsilly (1)
> **Documentation:** the documentation (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Cross-References:** later in (1)

### 6. 5. Iterating with Loops

#### For loops: Sequential

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When we talk about programming computers it's always with the goal of making the machine do something that we're not so good at, or just don't want to do.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=8)** One of the things that a computer can do really well is the same thing over and over again.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=13)** What I'm talking about is called looping or iterating.

>

> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=50)** It's very typical when you see for loops in the wild for the variable to be called i for index.

>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=66)** This is a convention people use, starting with zero.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=70)** It's not required, but because it's very common, maybe the most common, that's what we'll use here.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=77)** So we'll start out with that initialization setting the variable i to zero, and then we'll add a semicolon.

> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=98)** We started with zero, so if we were to go through until our counter equals 10, that would actually be 11 times through the loop or 11 iterations.

> **[9:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=564)** It doesn't clear the previously set values.

> **[9:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=568)** Now if I hit Return, I can see all the ones that we are not, up until we reach the one that's correct and then the loop stops.
>
> **[9:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=576)** One more quick note about this code.
>
> **[9:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=579)** As programmers, we very much like not to repeat ourselves.
>
> **[9:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=583)** So you may look through this block of code as you're writing and say, "Ah, look at this.
>
> **[9:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=588)** I have this little piece where I'm getting into that array by index, and I'm doing that three times."
>
> **[9:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=595)** This is ripe for rewriting.
>
> **[9:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=597)** So what we can do is every time through the loop we can set a variable which could be called current page title and set it to page names sub i.
>
> **[10:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=608)** Then we can rewrite our comparisons so they use current page title instead.
>
> **[10:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=615)** This is nice because not only are we not having to rewrite this somewhat obscure-looking construct several times, but because we've given this a variable with a descriptive name, it makes it very clear what we're doing when we use it in these subsequent lines.
>
> **[10:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=631)** That just makes it a little easier to read what's going on.
>
> **[10:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=634)** In a block of code this short, that's not really a big win, but it's something.
>
> **[10:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=638)** And helping yourself out by using meaningful variable names instead of something like page names sub i, maybe just a little bit less meaningful can be a great help.
>
> **[10:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=648)** Especially as your program builds in size over time.
>
> **[10:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-sequential?u=76281980&t=652)** And that completes our first look at using sequential for loops to do the same thing over and over again.

#### For loops: Enumerative
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980)

> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980&t=314)** Without going too far into the weeds, JavaScript is an object-oriented language that uses a model called Prototypal Inheritance.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980&t=322)** There's a video about this later in the course, but for our purposes right now, it means that JavaScript can be modified very deeply.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980&t=330)** And an object like this could have inherited properties from other parts of code that we can't see in this particular file.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980&t=338)** And those could potentially show up when you try to enumerate the object.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980&t=343)** Using this "hasownProperty" line is your way of safeguarding yourself against enumerating things that you didn't mean to.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980&t=350)** As I mentioned, the details of this are a little on the esoteric side for the moment, and there's certainly a lot of code out there where you won't run into this problem at all.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980&t=359)** But if you're going to be enumerating over an object, it's pretty important to include this check.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980&t=364)** So just file that away and know that it's something to look out for.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980&t=368)** And if you ever see it, in the meantime, you'll know what it means.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/for-loops-enumerative?u=76281980&t=372)** So that is how you can use Enumerative for loops to iterate over both arrays and objects.

#### Set and Map: Newer loopable data types
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the last video, we looked at arrays and objects as data types that can be used for looping.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=5)** At this point, I want to pause briefly to introduce a couple other data types that have been added to JavaScript more recently which can store data in a similar way but act differently in ways that impact loops.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=17)** Set and map.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=20)** Set and map are special types of objects.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=22)** To create one, we use the "new" keyword, which gives you a new instance of the type of object it's given.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=29)** Using new to create a new instance is a common paradigm in object-oriented programming in many languages.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=35)** After the object name you include parentheses which can be empty to create an empty instance of the object, or some data can be included to pre-populate your object as long as those data are passed in the correct format for the type of object you're creating.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=49)** Creating an object this way is called using its constructor function.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=53)** All JavaScript data types have constructors that one could use, though in my experience, they're rarely used for things like arrays or objects, let alone numbers and other basic types.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=64)** Sets behave very similarly to arrays in that they both store lists of values.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=69)** The main obvious difference is that a set will store each value exactly once.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=74)** If you try to insert the same value more than once, any time you try after that first successful time, nothing happens.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=82)** Unlike arrays, direct access to the items in the set by index or subscript isn't possible.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=88)** You have to use methods, special functions that are part of the set object to interact with its items.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=94)** To add an item for instance, you use a method called appropriately, "add."
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=99)** Set doesn't actually include a get method that would be analogous to subscripts or indices on an array.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=105)** They're meant for storing a bunch of unique items, potentially looping over those items and checking if an item exists in the set.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=112)** The has method shown here will tell you if that string "juno" exists in my set.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=118)** A map behaves a lot like a standard object, storing pairs of keys and their values.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=124)** The main differences for our current purposes are that unlike an object, a map remembers the order that its keys and values were inserted.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=133)** When looping over a map, the keys and values will always come back in the same order.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=138)** Their behavior is predictable in this regard.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=141)** Just like with sets, items and a map need to be accessed using a special set of methods like "get" and "has" instead of via direct access by property name the way you can with an object.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=152)** This makes using them a little less convenient for basic stuff than plain objects.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=157)** There are other differences between sets and maps, arrays and objects that you might find useful as you start writing more code of your own.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=164)** But I'll leave it to you to explore those differences further on your own.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=168)** Now let's take a look at a bit of code so we can see what these data types look like in practice.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=174)** Let's look at sets first.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=177)** We'll start with an array where you'll notice the first item is repeated.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=182)** Creating this with an array literal like this is pretty quick and easy.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=186)** To do the same thing with a set, we might use the add method to add each item.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=191)** So save myself a little typing.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=192)** I'm doing a little copy and paste here but I can just call my set and add one.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=199)** And then if I try to do this again we can see that the set doesn't change.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=204)** There's no error thrown, but nothing changes.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=207)** So I can do this over and over again to add all the items that are in that array but it gets a little tedious.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=214)** Fortunately, there is a handy shortcut.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=217)** If you pass an array into the set constructor, it will be turned into a set for you, removing all the duplicates without any extra effort.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=226)** So with this line here, I'm creating set two which is calling the set constructor, but passing in the original array as the data to initialize it with.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=239)** So now if I look at set two, its contents are the same except the duplicates are gone.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=244)** Sets are clever enough to remove those duplicates no matter where they are in the list.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=249)** Once the items are in, we can use the has method to check if an item is actually in that set.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=258)** So I can see if my set has the number one or I can check if it has 12, which returns false.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=268)** To loop over the items in a set easily, we can use a for of loop.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=276)** Just copy and paste this here.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=278)** We'll use my set two for this because it has everything.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=282)** There we go.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=282)** It loops over every item and returns their values logging the message to the console.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=287)** Now let's check out a map compared to an object.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=291)** Clear the console first and here is a bird object with a genus, species and common name.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=300)** Creating an object is very simple using an object literal like this.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=304)** To create a map, we use its set method, after we initialize it, of course.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=312)** That set method has two strings in the parentheses, the key a comma, and then the value for that key.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=321)** These three lines would create the equivalent map to the bird object.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=327)** This is pretty verbose compared to an object literal.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=330)** If you want the order that the items were inserted to be meaningful, though, this extra bit of verbosity should be worthwhile.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=337)** So I'll copy and execute these lines, and that results in a map with the same structure as our original object.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=346)** We can use the get method on a map to retrieve a value by its key, which is not possible with a set.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=353)** Maps do also have a has method that will tell you if a key exists in the map but it doesn't work on values.
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=361)** So has "genus" will return true because there is a key called genus.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=369)** But if we try the same thing on this value, which is a part of the map but isn't a key, that's going to return false.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=378)** If we loop over the items of the map using a for of loop you'll see something a little different than we see with a set.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=387)** It returns a series of arrays, each containing the key and value.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=392)** It does not just return the values.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=395)** A list of arrays in this form is called an entry list and you can get it from a map using its entries method, which looks like this.
>
> **[6:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=403)** So it's very similar to what we get from this for loop, just formatted a little differently.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=409)** Now as it happens, objects can also be turned into entries using the global Objects.entries method.
>
> **[6:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=417)** Object with a capital O is a built-in global variable containing, among other things, methods that can manipulate objects.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=425)** So if we call Object.entries on our bird object, our original just regular bird object, not the map, we get back an array of arrays.
>
> **[7:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=438)** This gives us a less verbose way to construct a map from an object literal.
>
> **[7:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=444)** So if we wanted to create one more bird map, we can pass this construct into the constructor function for the map, and that creates a bird map from the object without having to use that set method over and over again.
>
> **[7:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=468)** That was a lot of pixels lit up about sets and maps.
>
> **[7:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=471)** Hopefully not too much.
>
> **[7:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=472)** Both are handy tools to have available.
>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/set-and-map-newer-loopable-data-types?u=76281980&t=475)** In my experience, you'll see arrays and objects more often in most people's code, but the extra features that these data types give you can prove useful.

> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=189)** So I do what's in here and finish by popping a value off of my list into my item.

> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=207)** But the last time I pop, and these are popping off the end, so the next time I do that, I'm going to get false.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=215)** And so when I check it, this condition is no longer true, and so the loop will stop, and the statements inside these braces don't execute.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=225)** If we did this with a for loop based on how long this array is, we'd still get the false value, but we go until we exhausted the whole thing, unless we included a break statement.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=237)** A while loop can express this intent a little more clearly.

> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=258)** When we start out, the list has one, two, three, four, five, six items, and it will keep going until we pop false.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=265)** Then it has five items, and then it has four, and the item that we get is false, and then we stop.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=275)** This really helps to deal with situations where you don't know the contents of a set of data, and you have a condition where you're sure you'll be able to break at some point.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=284)** But here's the danger with while loops, really with any loop, but I think it's a particular danger with while loops, it's easy to create what's called an infinite loop.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=293)** That is one that never breaks or stops.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=297)** Here's a very contrived example of an infinite loop.

> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=301)** We start with a counter of one.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=303)** So far so good.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=305)** Then we have a while loop whose condition is true.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=309)** Nothing is going to change this, it's just true.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=313)** And so every time through this loop, we would log a message to the console saying what value is in our counter.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=321)** And then we increment it.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=323)** Now, I've included a break statement here to make sure that if you happen to copy and paste this, it won't just go ad infinitum, so it will stop.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=333)** But if you were to comment this out or remove it, every time this loop executes the counter is going to get incremented, but the condition is always true.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=342)** So this will just keep going and keep going and keep going.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=346)** It will just print out numbers until there are no more numbers to print out, your machine, or at least your browser or node might run out of memory, or it might say, hey, your script is running away, would you like to abort?
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=358)** Thankfully, with modern computers, we don't have to worry so much about infinite loops taking down the entire system, but it's still something you want to watch out for because it can certainly be a pain to debug.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=369)** There's one more formulation of the while loop that I'd like to show you which is called the do while loop.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=375)** Here we have my list again with its list of booleans.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=380)** We're going to start my item out with a value of false, and the while condition says do some stuff while my item is not false.
>
> **[6:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=391)** With a normal while loop everything inside here would not execute because my item starts out as false, and we have said, while my item is not false, there might be some things to do.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=407)** But with this do statement, we are guaranteed that no matter what the condition is for the while loop, everything in here will be executed at least once that very first time.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=418)** So if there's a situation where you're writing a while loop, and you want to make sure that everything in the curly braces is executed at least that one time, you need to use a do while loop instead of just a regular while loop.
>
> **[7:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=431)** So now you've seen the while loop and its variation the do while loop.
>
> **[7:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=436)** Yet another way of iterating in JavaScript.
>
> **[7:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=438)** Use it with caution because sometimes you could end up creating an infinite loop without meaning to, but it's a great way to do some looping when you don't want to loop over a whole set of data, and you want to make sure your break condition is clear.
>
> **[7:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/while-loops?u=76281980&t=452)** There's one more form of looping to be discussed later, but first we need to talk about functions.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** case, (2), let (2), this, (2), raise (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (3), node (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is called (1)
> **Warnings:** watch out (1), caution (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)


### 7. 6. Functions

>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/basic-functions?u=76281980&t=158)** In order to use this handy new function, we do what's called invoking the function, which is to write out its name followed by parentheses and an optional semicolon.

>

> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/basic-functions?u=76281980&t=211)** This might look like I'm just rearranging things a little bit, and for now, you can think of it that way.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/basic-functions?u=76281980&t=216)** Instead of just creating the function directly in this expression, I'm setting a variable and assigning to that variable the function declaration.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/basic-functions?u=76281980&t=226)** So the name goes here on the variable instead of after the keyword function.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/basic-functions?u=76281980&t=232)** But defined this way, it will work almost exactly the same way.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/basic-functions?u=76281980&t=236)** You just need to make sure that you've defined it before you try to use it.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/basic-functions?u=76281980&t=241)** As we'll see in upcoming lessons, this is not nearly everything you can do with functions, but even if it were, this by itself would be worth knowing.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/basic-functions?u=76281980&t=249)** Functions at their most basic can tie together lines of code and give you one executable unit that has its own name and can be executed as many times as you need.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/basic-functions?u=76281980&t=258)** It's really useful.

> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=161)** And after the error, we return from the function, that means to finish and optionally deliver back some kind of result.
>

>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=181)** If this line executes, the rest of the code in the function, which is also known as the function body, is ignored.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=189)** So assuming speech is a string, we continue and replace all lowercase and uppercase "R"s using regular expressions and then return the modified speech.

>

>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=260)** We'll see this again later but when I say a method in this context, I mean a function that's part of an object.

> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=265)** Here, replace is a function defined on the Global string object.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=270)** So when I have a string, that method becomes available.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=274)** Okay?
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=274)** That's how you can take data and process it using a function.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=278)** Now we'll address the second current use case where we define a function that can return an answer to a question for frequent reuse.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=286)** For example, we know that we can determine whether a number is odd by using the modulus operator.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=292)** So 12 mod 2, for example, return zero.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=297)** And that's how we know that the number is even.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=300)** Using that expression directly doesn't make clear what we're trying to do at a glance.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=304)** It's preferable to create a function with a descriptive name just as we do with variables.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=309)** In this case, I'm checking whether a number is odd or even so I could define a function called "isEven" and return true for even numbers, false for odds.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=319)** Here's what that might look like.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=321)** We can call it a function "isEven" and we'll pass it a number, give us a little more space here, and then we could say if that number, mod 2 is equal to 0, we will "return true".
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=340)** And if not using "else", we'll "return false".
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=345)** Invoking this function with a name that we've defined is much easier to understand than trying to parse out a mathematical expression, even one that's pretty simple.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=356)** My general guidance for myself and anyone else is don't be shy about creating lots of functions.
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=361)** Indeed, there are ways of programming that result in nearly every single line being wrapped in a function.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=366)** How deep into that you go is like so many things, personal preference.
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=371)** Before we totally leave this function alone, it could be shortened to just an expression we're evaluating.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=378)** I'll show you what that looks like down here.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=380)** The condition we're checking evaluates on its own to true or false.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=384)** So instead of evaluating it and then returning true or false separately, we can just return the expression result directly.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=392)** You can choose whether you prefer this construction in the code you write, but you should know that it's possible and something many folks will use.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=400)** Now, we've seen two ways that functions can come in immediately handy when you're working with your JavaScript.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/arguments-and-parameters-in-functions?u=76281980&t=405)** One is to process data and return back the modified version and the other is to package up a way to ask a question and get back an answer to it over and over again.

>

>

>

>

>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=110)** That will give you a number between zero and one.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=114)** Multiplying that times a number will give us a number that's between zero and one less than that number we provide, which in this case is five.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=124)** We use math's floor method to round that number down basically trimming off anything after the decimal point.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=131)** And finally we add one, ending up with a number that is between one and in this case five.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=138)** Once we have that random number, we're just running a switch statement.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=142)** And for any of these five numbers that might be produced by that random number generator, we're changing the form of calvin into one of these five forms, tyrannosaurus, grey wolf, and so forth.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=154)** This algorithm could be modeled differently and made shorter, but this way keeps everything pretty clear using what we've learned so far.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=161)** Now we need to notice something about this function.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=163)** There's no return statement at the bottom.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=166)** Why is that?
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=167)** The reason is that the argument that's being passed in is an object and objects are passed into functions differently from other types of data.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=175)** Instead of a simple value being passed in a reference to the value is passed in.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=180)** And when I say a reference, that means the object I've created has a location in the computer's memory, and when I pass it into this transmogrify function, I'm passing in not a copy of the object that's totally separate from the original but a copy of the object's location in memory.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=198)** Then in my function, these lines that change the form are actually operating on the original object because the Calvin parameter points to the data referenced by the original calvin object.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=211)** This all generally follows the programming model of function parameters being passed by reference as opposed to being passed by value.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=218)** Stripping away the subtleties with references you're acting on the original data and with values you're dealing with copies.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=225)** So what happens when I run this transmogrifier function and pass calvin into it?
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=229)** As we saw he starts out as a human boy, and then after having run it, we would check this calvin value and see that the form has changed.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=240)** This is important because there may be times when we actually don't want the original object to be modified.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=246)** You might want a copy to come back out of this function instead.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=249)** So in that case, we would have a version of the function where instead of passing a calvin object in as we did previously and just modifying it in place, we might want to set up a new calvin object that gets returned from the transmogrifier leaving the original alone.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=265)** And I have a version of the function that does just this.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=269)** There's a definition of the calvin object up here at the beginning of the file and I'm going to just change this to newCalvin and copy it and bring it in here to Node.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=281)** I'll just clear the console with Control + L, paste it in.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=286)** The other version of this function is called transmogrifyCopy.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=291)** We'll pass in newCalvin to this function.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=297)** And so we can take a look at the original newCalvin which remains a human boy, but the result of the transmogrifyCopy function is a new object where he has taken the form of a grizzly bear.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=311)** So this version does not modify the contents of the original variable.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=316)** Going to minimize the terminal.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=320)** And we'll split this editor so we can look at the two functions side by side.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=325)** Here's transmogrifier and here's the transmogrifier Copy.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=329)** As we can see, a lot of the code is the same.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=331)** We start with the same type checking in both.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=334)** We have the same random number generator.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=338)** But in the copy function we're saving a copy of the form here, calling it newForm.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=346)** And then after we have our random number generator instead of modifying the calvin object directly we're just changing this newForm variable.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=354)** It's one of these various animals.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=357)** And then at the end, we create an entirely new object by copying in the values of the name and bestFriend properties into this new object, and the form is the newForm.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=370)** And then when it comes time for the function to end, we just return that new object which has the same name, same bestFriend, but the new form.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=379)** That's one way we can get a copy out of this function instead of modifying the original object.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=385)** Now note that this business of passing a copy of a reference is not just for objects that are clearly objects.
>
> **[6:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=393)** Ones that look like this object literal up here.
>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=396)** This principle also applies to arrays which you may remember are really objects in JavaScript.
>
> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=401)** So if you pass an array into a function and you modify that array, you're modifying the properties of the original object.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=408)** Always keep that in mind.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=409)** So to sum up, when you're working with functions and objects, be careful about whether you're passing in and modifying the original or whether you want to get a copy back instead.
>
> **[6:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/objects-references-and-functions?u=76281980&t=419)** Write your functions accordingly to make sure that you're getting back what you expect.

#### Jargon: Scope in JavaScript
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It's time for a brief digression to deal with a little jargon you might hear about.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=4)** JavaScript is super popular and draws a lot of attention from developers of many skill and experience levels.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=10)** So knowing where some jargon comes from can help you with possibly boring parties.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=15)** Here, we're going to talk about scope.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=17)** Scope refers to where variables are defined and what parts of our code can access the variables that we define.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=25)** Often when we talk about scope, we differentiate between global scope and local scope.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=30)** Global refers to variables that are defined in such a way that they are accessible anywhere.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=35)** Local means that a variable is accessible in a way that's more limited, perhaps only inside a particular function.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=42)** One piece of guidance almost every beginning programmer gets is to avoid creating global variables.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=48)** Global variables are accessible everywhere, which is very convenient, but the global name space as it's often called, is a resource that's shared among all scripts that are in use at any given time, and there may be a lot of those variables.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=62)** Global variables can be accessed, but also modified by any piece of code.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=66)** So your little island that you're working on could have bugs introduced by someone else's code if you're both trying to use a global variable with the same name.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=75)** Unfortunately, in JavaScript it's easy to create global variables.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=78)** If I just create a variable like this with no var keyword in front of it, it will always be a global variable.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=85)** Unless, I've enabled strict mode by putting this string at the top of my file, in which case, I'll get a warning, but the point is, it's easy to do this accidentally.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=95)** To properly create variables, JavaScript uses the keywords, var, let, and const.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=101)** With var, you create a variable whose scope is determined by the function in which it's defined.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=106)** This will keep the variable in local scope about which we'll talk more in the next video.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=112)** In ECMAScript 2015 and above, there are other keywords that can create locally-scoped variables, let and const.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=119)** These also create local variables, but they can be scoped inside blocks of code, even down to something as small as an if statement.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=127)** Basically, their scope is delimited by their enclosing curly braces.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=132)** With let, a variable you create can be reassigned, much like var.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=136)** With const, you create a variable that can't be changed, although sneakily, an object created as a constant can potentially have its properties inside changed.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-scope-in-javascript?u=76281980&t=145)** We'll get into more detail about scope with upcoming code examples.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** var (4), let (3), const (3), function (2), super (1)
> **Definitions:** refers to (2), means that (1), is a  (1)
> **Cross-References:** in the next (1)
> **Warnings:** warning (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/functions-and-scope?u=76281980&t=140)** If I hadn't used var to define it, I would've been resetting the value of the original global variable, not the one that's inside the function.

#### Writing shorter functions with arrows
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Writing functions doesn't take a ton of keystrokes, but in this video we'll explore arrow functions, which are part of ECMAScript 2015 that allow functions to be defined with fewer keystrokes.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=12)** JavaScript is very function-oriented.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=15)** Indeed, you can use it in a formally functional way, which pretty much means everything would be done via functions.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=21)** As a result, being able to define a function with less typing is pretty handy.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=26)** Let's look at this speech function that prints a few animal noises.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=31)** This is written as a function expression assigned to a constant called speak.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=36)** This is just like what we've seen previously.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=39)** We have our function keyword.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=41)** It's unnamed because we're assigning it to this variable and then we have our curly braces with the things we want it to do.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=47)** With an arrow function, we can save even having to type that word function.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=51)** We just leave it off.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=53)** But in order for this to be recognized as a function, we need to add an equal sign followed by a greater than sign, which looks a little bit like an arrow.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=64)** Otherwise, it's the same.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=67)** So in a nutshell, that is an arrow function, but there are a few more details that we'll look at.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=72)** These arrow functions aren't always the absolute shortest way to define a function, at least not immediately.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=80)** In this example, we have an is even function declared and it is quite short.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=84)** To rewrite this as a basic arrow function, we actually end up with a few more characters.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=88)** You can sort of see this sticking out a little bit further on its line.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=93)** However, along with being able to remove the word function, you can potentially remove even more.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=99)** If the function is only returning a result, not requiring any other work on more lines of code, you can leave off the curly braces and the word return.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=110)** That results in a one-liner.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=114)** So our parameters are here in a set of parentheses.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=117)** We have our arrow and then here's the expression that should be returned.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=122)** We have no word return.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=125)** If you're doing other work than just returning something simple, you'll want to keep those curly braces in and actually return something with the return keyword.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=133)** For what it's worth, this form took me a little while to get used to as a longtime user of the previous method of defining functions, and yet this still is not the absolute shortest it could be.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=145)** If there's only one parameter, you can even leave off the parenthesis here.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=151)** The code formatter that I have installed in this code space, prettier, prefers to keep the parameters, even if they're optional, which is why I have this prettier ignore comment here before that line.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=162)** I prefer to leave the parenthesis in for readability, but you'll probably see the absolute shortest version like this in code out in the world, so it's important to know how to parse it.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=173)** Arrow functions are a handy way to write functions with the fewest keystrokes.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=176)** They can be used for nearly anything a regular function expression can be used for.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/writing-shorter-functions-with-arrows?u=76281980&t=181)** They have a couple subtle differences from regular functions that will be worth looking into as you gain more experience, but for now, they're a useful feature you can expect to see in the code of others and most likely in your own, as well.
