#### Three little dots collect the rest
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In our efforts to become conversant in JavaScript, there's a bit of newer syntax that we might see that's going to appear a little bit mysterious.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=8)** It looks like an ellipses, but it's actually three dots typed as three periods or full stops in the parenthesis of a functions declaration.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=19)** This is called rest parameters, and the result is an array of arguments.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=24)** Previously, we've seen the array like object called arguments but this is an actual JavaScript array.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=31)** So along with the length property it has all the methods that regular arrays have for sorting and otherwise manipulating the items.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=38)** That might not be something you'll put into use very often but it's good to know.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=42)** Here's the adding machine function we saw previously rewritten to use rest parameters.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=47)** Because it's the only parameter in the function, all arguments are captured in terms here and can be looped over just like we did previously with the arguments object.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=58)** For simple cases like this, neither one is better or very much different.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=63)** The main difference with rest parameters is that you can use them in addition to regular positional arguments.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=69)** Here's a bake function for cakes with named parameters for temperature and time and a rest parameter for flavors.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=77)** So let's copy this and bring it into Node.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=82)** Now we can try running it a few ways, save a little typing, I have these ready to copy and paste.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=90)** I can run it with everything, including many flavors.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=96)** And if we compare the result to what we're expecting in the function body, we can see that it's going to say let's bake the cake for the temperature degrees for the amount of time in minutes.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=111)** Then here's our rest parameter for flavors and if there are any in the array, it's going to log a message with all of them included.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=119)** You can see it comes through as a regular array.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=122)** And by comparison the arguments object contains everything that was passed in including our named parameters.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=130)** So that's what it looks like if we include everything.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=134)** If we include just one flavor, note that this time, even though there's only one extra parameter, flavors here is still an array.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=145)** It's just an array with one item.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=147)** And of course, if I run this with no parameters my default values are used for temperature and time and flavors and arguments are both empty.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=157)** This construction allows me to provide names for parameters that I'd like to have names for, but provide additional options if I want them.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=165)** I could do this with the arguments object but that will include every argument named or not.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=170)** So if I wanted to do something only with the extras, I would've to remember to skip the first two arguments like we saw up here.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=178)** With rest parameters, I don't have to make any special effort.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=181)** There's a little something extra I want to mention in this function as well.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=185)** It's here on these first two console lines.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=189)** They're using a different way to build a string which is called a template literal.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=193)** These are delimited by these back ticks which are in the upper left corner of American keyboards to the left of the number one, but might also be found in the upper right or elsewhere depending on your keyboard layout template.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=206)** Literals make it easy to insert other values into a string as we're doing here with the temp and the time parameters.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=214)** Inside those back ticks, you just include included dollar sign and curly braces to set apart your item of interest.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=222)** The second message also uses slash "n" to insert a carriage return in the output so all the messages don't appear one right after the other.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=231)** You can see that right here.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=233)** With rest parameters, you have all the power of the arguments object we've seen before with the flexibility of letting it contain only the subset of parameters you want to collect together.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=243)** The fact that it's a real array, might also come in handy.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/three-little-dots-collect-the-rest?u=76281980&t=246)** The three dot syntax comes up in other places in JavaScript so it can be a little tricky at first, but this version is very common and relatively easy to understand.

### 8. 7. A Few More Advanced Pieces

#### Asynchronous code: Waiting is the hardest part
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the next several videos, we're going to talk about some pieces of JavaScript that are more on the advanced side, but that I think are important enough to introduce conceptually.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=9)** In this video, we're going to talk about the term asynchronous.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=13)** When we think about giving instructions to a computer, we generally think linearly.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=18)** Here's step one, followed by step two, and so forth.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=22)** We don't move on to step two until step one is completely finished.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=26)** This is very easy to follow conceptually, and a lot of code that we write follows this mode of thinking, which is all fine and good.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=33)** Code written this way is said to be synchronous with every step blocking the ones that come after it.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=38)** Step three doesn't run until step two is totally finished and so on.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=43)** But what if, say, step four is a step that takes a while and involves waiting for something that steps five and those after don't need right away?
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=54)** In order for the entire task to be complete, step four does need to be finished, but it doesn't need to be finished before step five can start.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=63)** If we're baking a cake, for example, we need to get that cake in the oven so it can bake, but we don't have to wait for the cake to be finished before we can make the frosting.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=74)** We need the cake and the frosting to be finished and cooled before we can put them together and call the whole job done.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=80)** But if we waited for the cake to be baked and cooled before even starting the frosting, it would just take much longer.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=88)** Code involving steps that can be run at the same time with the results of some steps coming in later is called asynchronous because some steps can be run in a way that doesn't block others.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=100)** The results will be needed eventually, just not immediately, so we can let other steps happen while waiting for the stuff that takes a while.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=108)** And when I say a while here, I mean it in computer terms.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=111)** In other words, a delay that a human might consider very brief, a couple of seconds, or even a fraction of a second can be slow enough that working asynchronously is noticeably better.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=120)** The main situation where we need to work asynchronously is when our code needs to load data from somewhere, usually somewhere on the internet.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=129)** This could be loading some inventory data from a store's back office, a photo from a catalog, anything where our code needs to make a request.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=137)** If you're using node, loading files from disk is an asynchronous operation by default because disks can be relatively slow.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=144)** We always assume that the network or other sources of data can be slow sometimes, and if all the code on a page had to wait for that data to load, everything would feel slow and unresponsive, which is bad.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=157)** As you start writing intermediate to advanced JavaScript code, you will almost certainly need to deal with various ways of writing asynchronous code.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/asynchronous-code-waiting-is-the-hardest-part?u=76281980&t=165)** In the next video, we'll look at some examples.

> **Code Keywords:** if, (1), let (1), while. (1)
> **Cross-References:** in the next (2)

#### Promises, async, and await
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to look at various approaches to writing asynchronous code, so you'll recognize these approaches in the wild.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=7)** We'll look at using callbacks, promises, and the new async and await keywords.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=13)** Here's some example code that uses these three approaches.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=17)** The first one uses the get method from the jQuery JavaScript library which is a function that takes a URL as its first parameter and a callback function as its second.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=27)** That callback gets the response from the server as its argument which you can then act on in the body of the function.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=35)** These calls are to [httpbin.org](httpbin.org), a real website that provides an API for testing just this sort of code.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=43)** This works fine for a single call but if there's another request that depends on the first one and maybe more after that, you end up having to nest all these callback functions within one another, and it gets to be a bit of a mess to write, and can be hard to understand if the functions get at all long.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=60)** Programmers call this sort of situation "Callback hell."
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=64)** Promises are a much more pleasant way to work.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=67)** Promises are objects that capture the result of an asynchronous action with a particular programming interface or API for handling when the promised data successfully comes through, or failed to, for some reason.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=81)** Axios is a popular library for making network requests and returning promises, which looks like this.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=88)** I make a request using Axios' get method, but then instead of a callback, I directly chain on a method called then, which is part of the promise specification, which then contains a callback where my data comes back.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=104)** This works because Axios get method returns a promise and promises have a method called then.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=111)** Inside that callback function, if I need to make another request, I can return another promise allowing me to chain another then method at the same level as the first one.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=122)** I know this might sound like a lot right now but the point is that this is how most of the better code involving network requests is written, and I want you to be able to recognize it when you see it.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=133)** Browser support isn't perfect, but there are lots of JavaScript libraries we can add onto our projects to polyfill or patch in support of the parts of the promise specification that your older browser of interest doesn't necessarily support out of the box.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=147)** But these days, most browsers support promises without any additional code needed.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=152)** And finally, there's async and await, which are part of ECMAScript 2017.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=158)** These make working with promises even easier to read.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=161)** Functions can be marked as asynchronous with the async keyword, and then network requests can have their return values stored directly instead of being handled inside a callback function.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=173)** All you need to do that is use the await keyword.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=176)** This can really shorten code that makes a lot of network requests, like get lots of things over here.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=182)** This is relatively new, and many developers are still coming to grips with it, but many browsers and the current version of Node all support this, so it's worth being aware of.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/promises-async-and-await?u=76281980&t=192)** So now you've seen some examples of asynchronous JavaScript in the form of old school callbacks, regular promises, and then the new async and await.

#### Object-oriented JavaScript: Prototypes and classes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Guide] In this video, we're going to talk briefly about object-oriented JavaScript because the term object-oriented appears often in programming, so it's good to have a bit of exposure to it and what it means for JavaScript.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=12)** We've seen JavaScript objects already.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=15)** They're a generic way to bundle up data that belong together, but when we talk about object-oriented programming, we're talking about the same thing but particularly at a concept level: being able to reuse code by setting a template for a type of data, and then every time I need to use it, getting an instance of that template.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=34)** Different languages handle this concept in different ways, especially in the way objects can be related to one another.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=41)** Let's say I wanted to make a cake template, which has some features like the number of layers, the primary flavor like chocolate, vanilla, or lemon, and some functions like bake and cool; all the things that any cake is going to need.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=58)** Anytime I want to make a cake programmatically, I could get an instance of my Cake object and be off to a good start just by writing code for whatever features are unique to that instance.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=70)** If I'm going to make a lot of chocolate cakes, I might want an object called ChocolateCake that inherits the shared features of the base object Cake but has its own features that are more specific than Cake but general enough to be reusable in all chocolate cakes, like making its primary flavor chocolate by default so I don't have to set it every time.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=90)** The relationship between the original object and other objects that are derived from it is called inheritance, and the way inheritance works is central to any object-oriented language.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=102)** So the question is what mechanism is used to relate these objects?
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=106)** The way JavaScript works is different from most other popular languages.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=110)** It uses a model called Prototypal Inheritance where every object has a link to a parent object from which it inherited some data and functions and that parent has the same link to its parent and so forth.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=124)** So ChocolateCake inherits from its parent, Cake, which inherits from the global Object.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=133)** These links are called __proto__ internally, and comprise what's called the prototype chain.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=141)** This topic is one that's worth looking into in more detail as you become a more proficient developer, but in terms of our goal of making you conversant and recognizing common code idioms, I want you to recognize something like this.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=154)** This is how you would add a method to an object using its prototype, which makes it available to all objects that use that prototype wherever they are down the chain.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=164)** In this case, we're adding a function called Bake which takes a temperature and a number of minutes.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=170)** Once it's defined this way, any cake or any chocolate cake would have access to that function and can invoke it the same way.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=178)** In ECMAScript 6, there's a way of building these objects that looks more like the syntax of other programming languages, particularly the class keyword.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=188)** Along with being a little less verbose looking, it allows developers with previous object-oriented experience to feel more comfortable in JavaScript.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=195)** That said, ultimately, this will still get turned into the prototype based declaration we saw before.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=202)** Because these are enhancements to the way the language looks but don't really change how it works underneath, additions like this are often called syntactic sugar.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/object-oriented-javascript-prototypes-and-classes?u=76281980&t=211)** There's a lot more to learn about object-oriented JavaScript if you're interested, but this quick overview gave you a few things to look for as you're developing familiarity with the language.

#### Jargon: Strong vs. loosely typed
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Programming languages are categorized in lots of ways, one of which is whether they're considered strongly or loosely typed.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=8)** This thankfully does not refer to how hard you hammer on the keyboard when writing your code.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=13)** JavaScript is loosely typed and I want to give you a little information on what that means and why at some point you might care.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=20)** I'm bringing up loose versus strong typing because it comes up with variables.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=25)** In JavaScript, you've seen that we can create a variable, assign it a number, and then turn right around and assign it a word, a string later.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=34)** Being able to reuse variables however you want makes JavaScript very flexible.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=38)** Very experienced programmers can have strong and different opinions on whether this is good, but either way, JavaScript is a loosely typed language.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=47)** In contrast with a strongly typed language, broadly speaking, your variables know about and enforce what kind of data they can store.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=55)** The language will trigger some sort of error if you try to say put a number where you originally had a string.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=61)** This can feel constraining, but it does help avoid bugs, especially in larger programs and applications.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=69)** Let's say you have a variable called author name that will be used later in some way that makes sense for a person's name.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=76)** If you later assign a number, your program might do something weird later because you fed it a number instead of a name.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=83)** A strongly typed language would flag this for you and note that it could be a problem.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=88)** One of the most popular solutions to this issue is TypeScript, which is JavaScript but with some extra features added on, like stronger typing.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=97)** Many large JavaScript projects and frameworks write their original code in TypeScript, and transform it into JavaScript for running on the web.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/jargon-strong-vs-loosely-typed?u=76281980&t=105)** So you may see this discussed or become curious about it at some point, and I wanted you to know about it.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Cross-References:** later in (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Warnings:** note that (1)

#### Modern JavaScript tooling
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we're going to talk about some of the popular tools in the JavaScript ecosystem that help with various common tasks.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=7)** When you're just starting out, you don't really need any of this stuff, but if you want to get into JavaScript professionally at some point, I want you to know about these tools so you can start exploring them in more detail.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=19)** JavaScript powers so many things on the web and elsewhere.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=22)** Some projects are enormous, involving millions of lines of code and are handled by teams of dozens or hundreds of people.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=30)** Open-source JavaScript projects can have contributors of many experience levels and all over the world.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=35)** With a continual maturation and proliferation of JavaScript, it was inevitable that developers would start writing tools to help solve various issues that come up.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=45)** One issue is how to handle JavaScript libraries and modules.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=48)** As projects grow, it makes sense to start splitting files into smaller ones that contain related code.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=54)** In an object-oriented project, there could be files for each type or class of an object or just a file containing all the helper functions you've written.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=63)** Once you've broken them up, you need a way to load the ones that you need.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=67)** Browsers load scripts with script tags, but managing those manually can be a pain.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=71)** What if script A doesn't work without script B having been loaded first?
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=75)** And oh, by the way, what if script B needs script N first and so on and so forth.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=80)** Until recently, there was nothing in the official JavaScript spec that specified how to handle loading shared JavaScript libraries.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=87)** That specification exists now called Dynamic Imports, but browser support isn't super good right now and the problem has been around much longer than the specification has existed so tools have been written to handle this.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=100)** Some of the most popular ones are probably Webpack, Rollup, and Vite.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=110)** Module loading is just a small part of what these tools can do.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=113)** They have plugins that can handle lots of other tasks like removing comments and white space from code so it takes up less space and can be loaded faster and tons more.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=123)** If you start using a JavaScript library like React or Vue, you'll almost certainly start using one of these tools.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=131)** There are also tools called Package Managers for managing the installation of JavaScript libraries.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=136)** If that doesn't sound important to you, think about a simple example, installing a jQuery slideshow plugin.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=142)** If you don't already have jQuery, you'll need it and then you'll need a slideshow plugin.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=148)** Then maybe you need a plugin to support touch input for mobile devices.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=152)** For something small, that's still a lot to download and deal with manually.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=157)** To help with this, there exists NPM and Yarn which handle downloading JavaScript libraries and packages, making sure to install everything you need in a standard folder structure.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=169)** Webpack, Rollup, and Vite are all aware of those standards as well, making dynamic importing of files installed this way very easy.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=177)** Learning NPM, the Node Package Manager, and Learning Package Management with Yarn are both courses that can help you a lot more with these topics.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=187)** One other kind of tool that's used all the time is a transpiler or compiler.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=191)** Babel is a transpiler that's wildly popular for converting ES6 and other more modern versions of JavaScript into ECMAScript 5, and TypeScript has its own compiler to convert it into vanilla JavaScript.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=205)** Either of these can be integrated with Webpack or Rollup or Vite, giving you a one-stop shop for building your finalized JavaScript when working on a larger project.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=215)** As I said earlier, you don't have to use any of these tools at first.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=219)** Depending on how you use JavaScript, you might not ever need to.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/modern-javascript-tooling?u=76281980&t=222)** But if you work with the language for a while, you might start to notice some friction in your workflow and tools like these might be able to help.

#### Responsible JavaScript
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=0)** - In this video, we're going to discuss responsible JavaScript use.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=5)** I think one of the first rules of JavaScript is to try not to use it.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=8)** That sounds weird, I know.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=10)** Depending on what you're trying to accomplish, there may be another tool that satisfies the need.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=15)** If you're trying to animate things on the web, for example, there are ways to do many kinds of animation entirely with cascading style sheets, CSS.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=23)** Sometimes with just a tiny bit of JavaScript to make them go.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=26)** If you want to look into this more, you can explore CSS transitions, transforms, key frame animations, page transitions, and more.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=35)** The courses, CSS: Animation and CSS: Transforms and Transitions can tell you a lot more about this.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=42)** Even HTML is pretty powerful on its own.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=45)** There are a lot of elements to make web forms, dialogue boxes, time and date pickers, sliders, and more.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=52)** These aren't necessarily glamorous, but they're useful and don't require JavaScript to work.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=57)** Keeping up with updates to web technologies can be, to be honest, a little exhausting, but it can also be pretty exciting, so try to watch for what's coming and what other folks are using and interested in.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=68)** Assuming that using JavaScript is the right call, and many times it is, don't get me wrong, you want to make sure you're using it for good and aware of how it's use will impact others.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=77)** JavaScript opens so many possibilities on the web and elsewhere.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=81)** In fact, it seems like there's little you can't do with JavaScript.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=85)** That being said, it can be argued that all this power has been weaponized and has implications for everyone, both those of us who write JavaScript and everyone who uses it on all our different devices.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=97)** First, there are the security risks.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=99)** JavaScript can be used to load data from anywhere on the web and can be injected into HTML tags that load data like image tags, and script tags, and so forth.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=108)** Attacks like these are called cross site scripting attacks or XSS, since they inject JavaScript that's not part of the site into a visitor's browser.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=117)** When authors become careless and leave these vulnerabilities, the results can be damaging.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=122)** Websites can be defaced, site visitors can have their personal information revealed or stolen, and more.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=128)** You can find out more about XSS in the course is [[Cybersecurity Foundations]]: Application Security and [[Programming Foundations- Web Security]].
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=138)** Next are performance issues.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=140)** Many websites, especially larger commercial sites, include JavaScript to fetch and display ads, and tracking scripts to gather information on their user's behavior and activities, some of which are quite invasive of our privacy.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=152)** These scripts are often large and of course need to be downloaded, which takes valuable time, include very many of them, and page load times start to go way up, resulting in slow, poor performance.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=164)** Keeping JavaScript lean is vital for good user experience.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=168)** Long downloads are annoying and having to run a lot of JavaScript can slow down a browser and drain phone and laptop batteries.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=175)** Beware of using lots of scripts, your visitors and customers time and attention is very valuable.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=180)** There's more information on optimizing websites for better performance in the courses [[Developing for Web Performance]], [[Vanilla JavaScript- Web Performance Optimization APIs]], and Learning Enterprise Web Application Performance, among others.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=195)** For these reasons and others, JavaScript can be blocked by users.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=198)** Sometimes entirely, often partially.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=201)** Content blockers on mobile and desktop browsers can try to block advertising and tracking scripts from ever loading, and some will take even more aggressive steps to try and protect user privacy.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=211)** There are institutional firewalls that may block some or all JavaScript, and of course, some users will simply opt out disabling JavaScript as a feature of their browsers.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=220)** Not many in the grand scheme of things, but enough to be aware of.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=223)** All of this is to say, feel free to use JavaScript, but be attentive and judicious about it.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=228)** When you're first learning, you don't have to think about these concerns too terribly much.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=232)** If you end up working in a job with JavaScript as part of it, these issues will be very important.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=238)** To recap, security, performance, and the fact that JavaScript can be blocked are all critical aspects of its use to be remembered.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=246)** So be careful when using JavaScript to display data users have entered.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=249)** Be mindful of how much JavaScript you're adding to a project.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=252)** Keep things as small and fast as you can manage.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/responsible-javascript?u=76281980&t=256)** And though JavaScript is exceedingly popular and available, keep in mind that not all users will have it available or turned on.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** css (4), html (2), xss (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (3), find (1)
> **Warnings:** be aware (1), be careful (1), keep in mind (1)
> **Code Keywords:** this. (1), require (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Speakers:** - in (1)


### 9. Conclusion

#### Continue your JavaScript development journey

> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/continue-your-javascript-development-journey?u=76281980&t=277)** It's a relatively brief course that will get you introduced to JavaScript running on the server instead of the browser.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/continue-your-javascript-development-journey?u=76281980&t=284)** JavaScript test-driven development, ES6, is a course that can introduce you to test-driven development.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/continue-your-javascript-development-journey?u=76281980&t=289)** A different way of working than many people might think of when first approaching a programming language.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/continue-your-javascript-development-journey?u=76281980&t=294)** It's also a really good way to work that's worth exploring so I'd recommend looking into the approach relatively early in your journey.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-the-javascript-language-22309208/continue-your-javascript-development-journey?u=76281980&t=301)** As with debugging, it will make the most sense after you've written a little bit of code to get your feet wet, but don't wait too long so you don't get too set in your ways.
>
